GERMANS AND HEREROS
HAVE NEW YEAR'S CLASH
Natives Routed by Kaiser's Troops
After Fierce Fight in South
west Africa.
BERLIN, Jan. 6.âFour companies
of German infantry, one of cavalry
and half a battery of artillery in Ger
man Southwest Africa spent part of
New Year's day in a fierce fight with
Insurgent Hereros that ended in a
bayonet charge through the under
growth and the rout of the natives.
Lieutenant General yon Trotha, in
telegraphing the news, did not give
the number of killed or wounded on
either side, but sayg the natives lost
heavily.

THE CALL
SAN FRANCISCO, SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1905.
BEEF TRUST
HAS INNING
IN COURT
Argument Presented
Before Supreme
Tribunal,
Held Suit Is Unprecedented
Interference Willi
Business.
Judge Holmes Interrupts Case to State
He Is Interested in Other Stock
yards Not Involved.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âArgument
In the case of Swift & Co. vs. the United
States, known as the beef trust con
spiracy case, was begun before the Su
preme Court of the United States to
day by Attorney John S. Miller of Chi
cago in behalf of the packers. He at
tacked the bill of the Government as
insufficient in its failure to allege facts
necessary to constitute a cause of ac
tion, and said that the charges made
were not facts, but conclusions of law;
that the commerce charged was not in
terstate nor foreign commerce, and that
even if it were interstate commerce the
facts given constituted no violation of
law.
He said that an injunction had been
prayed for and secured from the United
States Circuit Court for the Northern
District of Illinois under the Sherman
anti-trust act. He contended that the
case presented an interference in busi
ness such as never before had been un
dertaken. The charges, he said, were
of the most general character and did
not specify time and place.
did not believe, therefore, that the
charges could stand at ali, and h<?
quoted the indictments in the whisky
case to show that charges much more
specific than were here made had been
regarded as insufficient.
FEARS COURT CONTROL.
"It is alleged," interrupted Justice
White, "that your clients' agents com
bined to refrain from bidding in order
to put prices down, and again agreed
to put them up. Do you think there
should have been a specification of hour
and place?"
"Yes, your Honor/V responded Miller.
"If the Government had the facts why
should it not have given them?"
"Did you ask for a bill <Sf particu
lars?" inquired Justice Harlan, and
Miller replied in the negative, saying
that the defendants had filed a de
murrer in the case.
Miller said that if the packing indus
try could be interfered with as pro
posed in this case, the manufacturing
industries could- be similarly regulated,
and "thus you will find," he added,
"the Federal courts regulating a con
siderable part of the commerce of the
country-"
He contended that there was no in
terstate commerce involved, because
the business of the packers, including
the purchase of cattle and the sale of
meat, was confined entirely to Chicago.
Explaining the economic condition,
Miller said the demand for fresh meat
wag fairly uniform, and owing to the
necessity for maintaining a proper sup
ply of an acceptable article, he said,
there should be some understanding
among the packers. This was, he said,
perfectly legitimate, and the Govern
ment itself might properly assume the
supervision. The cattle supply was
not so regular as the demand, and to
this fact Miller attributed the fluctua
tion in prices.
HOLMES HOLDS STOCK.
At the beginning of Miller's present
ation of the case he was interrupted
by Justice Holmes, who said he held
stock In the Union Stockyards in Chi
cago and also in the Kansas City yards,
and inquired if they were involved in
the case. Miller replied that they were
not.
When Miller concluded Attorney Gen
eral Moody began. It was idle, he
said, to discuss the contention that the
charges of the bill are not connected
one with another. Be that as it may,
he added, the purpose of the suit was
single, and all the separate parts of
the bill were Interdependent for the
accomplishment of that end.
He also maintained the constitution
ality of the bill, and asked for the
books of the packers. Moody had
scarcely begun when the court ad
journed until Monday.

COLORADO'S
LAWMAKERS
TURBULENT
c
Exciting Scenes At
tend tiie Joint
Session,
House Speaker and Lieu
tenant Goyernor Both
Claim the Chair.
Pandemonium Reigns When Ruling
of One Presiding Officer Is Set
Aside hy the Other.
DENVER, Jan. 6.âBehind locked
doors and amid scenes of great confu
sion and excitement the Colorado Leg
islature to-day passed a resolution cre
ating a committee of fifteen members
to canvass the vote castf for Governor
at the last election and submit a report
to the Legislature not later than 2
o'clock on the afternoon of next Mon
day. The committee is composed of
five Senators and ten Representatives,
the political division being twelve
Republicans and three Democrats. It
organized this afternoon by the election
of Senator Arthur Cornforth of Colo
rado Springs as chairman and will com
mence the hearing of evidence to-mor
row.
The trouble began with the first ses
sion of the joint session and continued
until its close. It was expected that
there would be a contest over the re
spective rights of Speaker Dickson and
Lieutenant Governor Haggott to pre
side over the joint session. The Speaker
based his opinion that it was his right
to preside on a section of the State con
stitution which says the#flpeaker shall
open and publish the%afteetlon returns
in the presence of a majority <tt both
branches of the Legislature. Lieuten
ant Governor Haggott construed his
right to direct the proceedings on a
statute which declares that in conduct
ing any contested election the General
Assembly shall convene in Joint session
and that the President of the Senate
shall preside unless he Is the contestee.
Legal advice was given to each gen
tleman that his position was correct
PANDEMONIUM REIGNS.
Lieutenant Governor Haggott was
assured that the wording of the statute
amply covered the case. Speaker Dick
son was advised by his attorneys that
the proceeding was not officially known
to be a contest, and that a provision
of the State Constitution always over
rides a statute when the two are in
conflict. When Lieutenant Governor
Haggott mounted the rostrum Speaker
Dickson was standing in front of the
center of his desk, and did not move.
The Lieutenant Governor advanced,
the two men smiled, bowed, shook
hands and then stood gazing into each
other's eyes. Neither man made a
move and their features hardened as
they realized the situation. Together
they turned to face the Assembly, the
Speaker behind the center of the desk,
the Lieutenant Governor at his left
hand. Gently edging up to the Speak
er, Lieutenant Governor Haggott gave
him a gentle "hunch" with his shoul
der, as If to shove him along. The
Speaker was immovable. The gentle
"hunch" was repeated and still the
Speaker held his ground and brought
down his gavel with a thump.
"The clerk of the House will call the
roll," he said.
Bang! went the gavel of the Lieuten
ant Governor. He is a powerful man
physically, and every time he brought
hig black gavel down the pitcher of
water on the Speaker's desk trembled
and the tumblers bumped and jingled.
"The clerk of the Senate will call the
roll," he shouted.
SCENES OF CONFUSION.
Clerk Harper of the House and Sec
retary Farwell of the Senate began to
call their respective rolls, while great
confusion arose in the House. Mem
bers laughed, cheered and shouted,
while Representative Street a Demo
crat, gave vent to the cowboy yell,
"Yip, yip, yip!" A few names had
been called when Representative Fre
wen, a "Wolcott Republican," shout
ed: "Mr. Chairman!"
"Mr. Frewen, responded the Lieu
tenant Governor, "you are out of or
der."
"Mr. Frewen," shouted the Speaker,
"you have the floor."
"Mr. Frewen," said the Lieutenant
Governor.
Bang! bang! went the Speaker's
gavel; boom! boom! went the gavel
of the Lieutenant Governor.
Frewen accepted the ruling of the
man who gave him what he wanted
and commenced to speak to a point
of order which was that the President
of the Senate was the proper presid
ing officer at a joint session.
"You are out of order, sit down,"
commanded the Sneaker.
"You have the floor," said the Lieu
tenant Governor, and again the gavels
banged.
"I move this session adjourn,"
shouted Senator Morgan.
"It is moved that this session ad
journ," shouted the Lieutenant Gov
ernor.

PET PARROT
SPOILS AS
ELOPEMENT
Awakens Family as
Girl Is Stealing
Out of House.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HOLTON, Kan., Jan. 6.âA green
parrot with eyes resembling new shoe
buttons was the means of spoiling the
romance of Miss Lena Anderson,
daughter of Nels Anderson, a farmer
seven miles northeast of town.
Olaf Olson, employed as a "hired
man" on the farm, has been paying
attention to the young woman, but
her parents have opposed the suit un
til he could show $78 in bank. Be
coming discouraged at the prospect of
waiting so long the young persons
planned to elope. Olson placed a
ladder against the window of the
room occupied by the girl last night
and awakened her by whistling. As
the girl prepared to descend the par
rot owned by her father, awakened
the family by screaming, "I seen yer,"
and the elopement was off.

PRICE FIVE CENTS.
Unabfe to Attain
Top of Social
Ladder.
LONDON, Jan. 6.âEnglish society
drawing-rooms will not see much of the
Duchess of Manchester for some time.
Those who knew her well are not sur
prised that she has decided to remain
for an indefinite period in her own
country. She had gone to enormous
expense in the hope of being able some
day to entertain royalty only to' be dis
appointed.
It was publicly announced that the
King and Queen were to be entertained
at Kylemore Castle when they visited f
Ireland, but they did not go there; in
fact, their Majesties carefully avoided
the West of Ireland, although it was
included in their original programme.
It was stated at the time that their
non-appearance at Kylemore was due
to the fact that the structural altera
tions at the castle could not be com
pleted in time, but it is now said that
they were delayed Intentionally, when
it was found that the King and Queen
were not going there, so as to escape
the inference that the Duchess had
been snubbed.
When it was announced some time ago
that the Prince of Wales proposed to
visit Ireland early this year, the
Duchess and her friends exercised all
their powers to get the heir apparent
to go to Kylemore, but Lord Ardilaun,
the big Dublin brewer, stepped in and
said in effect: "No, George, you must
come and stay with me." And so the
Prince is going to put up at Ashford,
a place not half so beautiful as Kyle
more.
There are several reasons put forth
to explain why the Duchess of Man
chester has never succeeded in getting
to the top of the social ladder. Disre
garding the old adage aoout doing in
RomÂ« as the Romans do, she has re
fused to participate in the gambling
games so popular in swell society, and
has been rather outspoken in express
ing her disapproval of the devotion to
bridge for high stakes displayed by
certain patrician dames who are in
high favor with the King.
It is said,"too, that when she first
came to England as the wife of the
Duke she assumed airs of superiority
that gave offense to the smart set who
were averse to taking lessons In pro
priety from a Cincinnati girl. Conse
quently they did their best to thwart
her social ambitions and are flattering
themselves that they have succeeded.
They say now that it Is pique which
has caused her to return to America
and take the Duke with her.
rl *
BRIAN GUEST
AT A BANQUET
MEMPHIS, Term., Jan. 6.âA banquet
at which 400 covers were spread was
tendered William J. Bryan to-night by
the Jackson Club of Memphis, Bryan
was introduced as the next President
of the United States. His subject was
"Watchman, What of the Night?"
After speaking of the causes which
in part contributed to the recent de
feat of the Democratic party, Bryan
said the platform adopted at Kansas
City expressed the views of more Dem
ocrats than any platform adopted
since. He praised several recommenda
tions made by President Roosevelt and
spoke of him as doing many good
things. Bryan closed as follows:
"Just now President Roosevelt gives
promise of beginning a reform move
ment. He recommends legislation
which will inform the public in regard
to campaign contributions. This is a
far-reaching recommendation and
should receive the earnest support of
every Democrat. He also recommends
an enlargement of the scope of the in
terstate commerce laws. This, too, is a
step in the right direction, and he
should receive the support of all Demo
crats in any efforts which he makes to
bring the railroads under a strict con
trol. If the President, with the aid of
the Democrats, accomplishes any re
form the public will secure <he benefit
of it; if, even with Democratic aid, he
fails, the educational work which he
does will help the Democratic party in
future contests.
"In municipal affairs the Democrats
are, as they should be, quite unanimous
in favor of municipal ownership of all
municipal franchises, and our party
must face the fact that a failure prop
erly to regulate the railroads will com
pel the public ownership of the rail
roads. If the Federal Government
operates only the trunk lines, leaving
the States to operate the network of
local lines, we can have public owner
ship without centralization, and, as I
believe, without danger to any public
interests."

NEW YEAR CLASH.
In Midst of Festivities Japanese En
gage the Russians.
' MUKDEN, Jan. 6.âThe Japanese
tried to combine business with pleas
ure, and while their new year festivi
ties were in progress on January 2
made an attack upon the village of
Vito, in front of one of their positions, j
The Russians replied with artillery on
.the right and the center, and prevented ]
the Japanese from occupying the vil-j
lage.
The same night a party of Siberian
volunteers surrounded the village of
rilnlounto-vvn, which the Japanese had
been using as a signal station. While
one column made an attack on the left
front, three others, in command of
Captain Toviansky, rushed the rear of
the village without firing and caught
the Japanese unexpectedly,driving them
out. Subsequently the Japanese opened
a heavy fire from the neighboring hills,
which compelled the Russians to retire
from the village. They carried off
twelve prisoners, seven of whom were
wounded. The Russian loss was nine
killed. It is not known to what extent
the Japanese suffered, but Judging by
â¢ the number of rifles abandoned ther
must have been many killed or
wounded.

CHRISTMAS IN RUSSIA.
â¢Jollity Not Affected by Fall of Port
Arthur.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. 6âThis is
the Russian Christmas eve. The streets
have been filled all day with crowds of
shoppers and the markets, overflowing
their customary bounds, filled adjoining
streets with booths and stalls, where
every variety of Christmas trees were
. piled high. Apparently the fall of Port
Arthur had little effect on the jollity
of the Christmas crowds. Promptly at
midniÃht everything closed up and the J
city became instantly dark and the
streets were deserted, but really for a
, renewal of the holiday festivities to
morrow.
A great service in honor of the dead
of Port Arthur will be held in the
Kazan Cathedral on Sunday.

JAPANESE USE SEARCHLIGHTS.
Employ Them for Purpose of Making

MUKDEN, Jan. 6.âThe night of
January 5 the Japanese turned search
lights on the Russian position at the

tions, but were received with a hot
rifle fire and driven back.

ot a Russian foraging party with Jap

foragers left their carts, drove back
the Japanese, took four prisoners and
secured the body of a dead Japanese
officer.

I MARKET STREET S
Both Nations Ready
to Continue the
Game of War.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âJapan has
made no overtures for peace to Russia,
directly or indirectly, through the
United States or any other power, and
contemplates no such action, and now,
that Port Arthur has fallen, purposes
to press the war in the north all the
more vigorously by reinforcing the
Japanese armies at Liaoyang with the
greater part 'Of the troops which have
been besieging Port Arthur.
This, in brief, represents the views
of Mr. Takahira, the Japanese Min
ister.
' The fall of Port Arthur,' said the
Minister, "is but a step in the war
which Japan is waging for a principle.
Certainly it is an important step, but
nothing could be further from the
truth than the assumption that be
cause Japan has captured a stronghold,
the fall of which has long been expect- 1
ed, the Japanese Government will now
make overtures for peace. Japan is too
busy lighting. We are as much in
earnest to-day as we were at the out
set of the war. We have made no over
tures for peace, either directly or Indi
rectly, nor have the powers approached
us with any idea of intervention."
At the Russian Embassy it was re
iterated that Russia would fight all the
harder in view of the temporary loss of
Port Arthur.
Europe, it is learned, is firmly of the
conviction that however dark the out
look for peace at this moment, when
the prospect brightens it is to Presi
dent Roosevelt that the neutrals as
well as the belligerents will look as the
intermediary through whom peace ne
gotiations will be initiated. As a Euro
pean Embassador said to-day, the
American Government is practically
the only Government to which both
belligerents will be willing to look for
assistance in reaching a settlement
when the time comes, and aside from
this fact the high personal regard in
which the President is held, both at
St. Petersburg and at Tokio, makes it
all the more probable that through
him, when Russia and Japan have
fought their fight, the powers must
hope for peace.
MAY FLOAT SHIPS.
No Official Inspection Yet Made at'
Port Arthur, However.
TOKIO, Jan. 6.âThe Navy Department
announces that the Japanese naval
force at Port Arthur has sunk the fol
lowing Russian war vessels in that har
bor: Torpedo-boats, the Gaidamak and
Fusatonik; torpedo-boat destroyers, the
Jetirny, Sisik and Boevoi.
The armored cruiser Bayan is lying
on the south coast of the harbor se
verely damaged and the gunboat Bobr
is entirely burned as the result of the
Japanese shelling.
The Russian battleships sunk at Port
Arthur have not as yet been officially
inspected. In those destroyed by the
Russians at tHe last moment there are
no signs of interior explosions. It is
hoped that the damage done to them
will be repairable. The docks are par
tially destroyed and filled In, the gates
also being damaged. The great crane
is intact and serviceable.

Port Arthurs Fall
Due to Lack of
Ammunition.

Baron Kleist, a Russian naval officer, 1
says lack of ammunition was the main
lactor in determining the surrender of
Port Arthur and that since September,
when the first general order was issued ;
announcing a scarcity, the greatest
economy was exercised.
Baron Kleist says the Japanese b>e
on the forts was so accurate that the
men regarded an order to serve in a j
fort as a sentence to death. Many died
without visible wounds, from the mere
violence of the concussion of huge Jap
anese shells, which made blood burst
from the eyes, mouth and nostrils of
those standing near. Early in the
siege the ranks of the Russian artil
lery were so depleted that Infantrymen
had to be employed to serve the guns, j
ANCESTRY OF STOESSEL.
Family Originally German, but Forced
From the Country.
OMAHA, Jan. 6.â J. S. Weidemeyer,
foreman of the Union Pacific shops
here, sets at rest a dispute which has
been going the rounds of the European
papers with respect to the ancestry of
General Anatole M. Stoessel, the Rus
sian commander at Port Arthur. Fore
man Weidemeyer studied mechanics in
Switzerland with two brothers of Stoes
sel. He said to-day that the claims of
both Germans and Swedes that the
hero of Port Arthur is of their nation
ality are In a sense right.
Tho Stoesseis, he declares, were jon
the losing side in the thirty years' war
and General Stoessel's ancestor was
forced to flee from Germany. He set
tled in Sweden, but his son went to
Russia and entered the army. For
many years in both Germany and Rus
sia many members of the family have
been military men.
RUSSIAN GUNS BOOM.
Turned on the Foe Lying Intrenched
Near Mukden.
GENERAL OKU'S HEADQUAR- !
TBRS, Jan. 6. via Fusan/âDuring the
last three days the Russian bombard
ment has been heavier than at any
time during the past six weeks. For two
days following the receipt of the news of
General Stoessel's letter to General
Nogi offering to surrender Port Arthur
hardly a. shot was fired, but since then
many heavy guns have been brought
into use and there is almost constant
firing.
There is no indication of the meaning
of this activity on the part of the Rus
sians. Apart from this conditions at
the front remain unchanged. The Japa
nese are using searchlights. . j
There is great rejoicing among the
Japanese troops over the fall of Port
Arthur, and celebrations take place
every night.

BISHOP SPALDING SUFFERS
SEVERE PARALYTIC STROKE
Distinguished Catholic Prelate Is Sud
denly Stricken and Friends Deem
His Condition Serious.
PEORIA, 111., Jan. 6.âBishop John
L. Spalding of the Roman Catholic
church to-day suffered a severe para
lytic stroke. His brother, Dr. Leonard
H. Spalding, is by his side continually.
The Bishop is 65 years of age, and
while he has been in fair health all
his life, his age tends to add to the
fear of his friends for another and more
serious attack. He complained this
morning that he was suffering with a
slight attack of neuralgia, but nothing
more was thought about it. About 4
o'clock his housekeeper was startled by
the sound of a fall in - the Bishop's
room. She ran hastily to the place and
found him lying on the floor, partially
unconscious and unable to rise.
At a late hour he' is resting com- |
f ortably.

BARD OPPOSES
THE MEASURE
California's Junior Senator
Argues Against Union of
Arizona and New Mexico
ADDRESSES COLLEAGUES
Gives His Assent to Proposi
tion to Consolidate Okla
homa and Indian Territory
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âSenator
Bard to-day occupied the entire time
of the* Senate given to the Joint state
hood bill.
Senator Bard presented a masterly
argument on behalf of Arizona in her
plea for the integrity of her boun
daries, that she be not forced to join
another Territory in order to become
a State. He followed the story of the
admission of Territories since Wiscon
sin and showed that in the case of
Arizona the act creating the Territory
distinctly proved, as it did in no
other case, that while its area might
be divided it could not be added to.
Bard long ago took the initiative on
the Republican side against this plan
of making one State of Arizona and
New Mexico together and has kept up
such a persistent and aggressive fight
that he lias come to assume the posi
tion of the leadership of the opposition
how that the bill is fairly before the
Senate. It is largely due to his efforts
that the lively interest of a number of
Republican Senators, including, lately,
Senator Foraker, has been enlisted
against it. During the delivery of his
speech this afternoon Senator Bard
held the closest attention of the Senate,
the chamber being nearly full and the
Democrats occupying what vacant
seats were to be found on the Repub
lican side.
At the conclusion the members crowd
ed about to shake Senator Bard by the
hand and congratulate him upon his
forceful espousal of the cause he has
at heart.
Bard began by reciting the history of
the pending bill, which had grown out
of earlier and separate hilts providing
for the introduction of Arizona and of
New Mexico as separate States, and
showing that the people of neither Ter
ritory had ever applied through their
Representatives or otherwise for state
hood other than as separate States. He
declared that no bill would come before
the session more important than this
statehood bill, affecting as it does the
destinies of a million of our American
people, and proposing to terminate the
control of Congress over the only con
tiguous territory belonging to the
United States.
Senator Bard said in part:
The creation of new States has often marked
some Important Â«poch In the political history
of our nation, and too frequently has signified
the accomplishment of some selfish scheme' of
a political part? which at the time controlled
the Government. - There does not appear to be
any circumstances by which either of the
great, political parties of this day can secure
any sure advantage by either the enactment
or defeat of this measure: and I believe that
the Senators cannot be persuaded to let any
hope for political advantage to either of the
parties, whose representatives are supposed to
be divided by the central aisle of this cham
ber, prevent them from considering this meas
ure only on the higher plane of duty to the
republic and to the people most directly Inter
ested In It. I have no objections to the pro
posed Joining of Oklahoma and Indian Territory
to make a State of the Union, but I believe it
would be more consistent with'the principles
of our Government to permit the people of each
of. the Territories, separately, to vote upon the
proposition and to require a vote of a majority
of the qualified electors of each Territory to
ratify the proposed constitution of the new
State.
Bard then recited the conditions ex
isting in the Territories, their immense
area and compared them In this respect
to their neighbors and to the Eastern
States. He recited the testimony of the
Delegates from the- Territories in pro
test, as well as their Governors and
representative citizens, who said that
the people were united in their desire
for statehood, but equally united
against It on the terms proposed.
Continuing Senator Bard said:
The Territories of Arizona and New Mexico
are in a chaotic state and entitled sooner or
later to become members of the Union of
States. If they are not yet prepared for
statehood Congress may Justly deny their ap
plication, but Congress cannot Justly unite
them if the proper political equilibrium of the
various sections of the country is to be pre
â¢â¢rved.
The people of Arizona, particularly, are
earnestly protesting against the passage Of
this, measure. Through the fear of conse
quences' and Injury which the bill would in
flict Â«'f>eh them, they have abandoned all hope
that Congress will at this time give Arizona
st i arate statehood, though such has been their
| afnbttton for a whole generation. In surren
j dering this hope now Arizona begs, but begs in
| a manner. that is dignified, though intensely
| earnest, that she may be spared the degrada-
I tion of the loss of her separate autonomy and
identity, and the humiliation of having her
boundaries forever effaced, and forgetting in
her distress the rights that she may claim, al
most pitifully says: "Rather than Incur the
impending disaster of a Joint statehood with
New 1 Mexico we request Congress to allow u<Â»
to remain as a Territory of the United States."
I On the conclusion of Bard's remarks
the bill temporarily was laid aside and
j the Senate went into executive session.
After the doors were reopened the
reading of the omnibus claims bill was
completed and after passing a few bills
oit minor Importance the Senate ad
journed until Monday.

"The session will not adjourn and
members will keep their seats," called
the Speaker.
SENATORS LEAVE HOUSE.
"All in favor of adjourning will say
'aye,'" called the Lieutenant Gov
ernor. "All opposed, 'no.' "
The "ayes" and "noes" came in
about equal volume and then down !
came the gavel of the Lieutenant Gov
ernor with a vicious slam.
"The ayes have it and the session is
dissolved."
Whack! went the Speaker's gavel.
"The noes have it and the session is ,
not dissolved," he said.
"The members of the Senate will
leave the House," said the Lieutenant
Governor, and quickly walking from
the Speaker's stand he strode up the
aisle waving his gavel over his head
as though he was leading an infantry
charge. |
All the Democratic Senators and a
number .of the Republican Senators
followed him.
Several Republican Senators re
mained in (he House and took part in
the proceedings held there until the
filial adjournment.
The doors were then locked and no 1
member was permitted to pass out.
Again and again Representative
Frewen raised the point of order that
no proper joint session was being held,
and every time the speaker declared
him out of order.
Representative Griffith then pre- '
sented the protest from Governor Pea
body, which was read by the clerk. It
covered the history of the recent elec
tion in Denver and suggested to the
Legislature that it was the duty of
that body to make a complete inves
tigation of the election. It protested i
against the canvass of the votes until
the investigation had been completed
and the number of legal votes for each
candidate had been ascertained.
GOVERNOR'S PROTEST READ.
Accompanying the protest was a res
olution presented by Griffith urging the
appointment of a committee of ten from
the House and five from the Senate to
investigate the vote and report daily to
the Legislature, with a final report not
later than Monday afternoon.
The reading of the Governor's protest
lasted but about a minute, when Rep
resentative Frewen raised the point of !
order that it was not germaln to any
subject under consideration. He was
declared out of order, raised his point
of order again and once more was de
clared out of order. He appealed from
the decision of the chair and the Speak- .
er refused to hear his appeal.
Representative Street moved an ad- â¢
journment and the Speaker would
entertain it. Street insisted that, hie
motion was in order according tc the
rules. He was informed by the Speak
er that this was a Joint session and no
rules had been adopted for the control
of a Joint session.
After the reading of the resolution
Griffith demanded a roll call. On its
adoption several members made
speeches explaining their views, the
Republican Senator Morgan declaring
that he only voted "aye" because he
did not wish to ally himself with the
Democratic party, but he wished to say
that the presiding officer of the Senate
had been insulted by the House. The
resolution was adopted by a vote of 51
ayes and d no, the negative vote being
cast by the Republican Senator Wood.
Seventeen Republican Senators were
present and voted for the resolution.
Throughout the roll call Representative
Street constantly urged the point of or- i
der that no quorum was present and ;
was constantly declared out of order.
Street finally sat down. After the
adoption of the Griffith resolution a
motion to dissolve the joint session was
carried. Speaker Dickson then called
the House to order and it promptly ad
journed.
Lieutenant Governor Haggott and his
followers assort to-night that five of
the Republican Senators who were in
the joint session to-day will remain
away to-morrow and prevent a
quorum.
The Wolcott Republicans, whose
leader is Lieutenant Governor Haggott,
have demanded certain concessions |
from the Peabody people as the price |
of their assistance. These things are
reported to be the privilege of naming
the two new Justices of the Supreme
Court and getting half the Guberna
torial patronage. The Peabody people
are not willing to-night to agree to the
request.

MISSOURI LEGISLATURE.
Famous "Alum Bill" Is First Measure
Introduced in the House.
JEFFERSON CITY, Mo.,' Jan. 6.â
"When the Legislature convened to-day
the first bill introduced In the House
was the famous "alum bill." This bill
is to repeal the law prohibiting the
use of alum In the manufacture of
baking powder. The statute was en
acted during the 1899 session of the

sulted in the indictment of several
Senators on the charge of having ac
cepted bribes to prevent the repeal of
the statute. During the investigation
Lieutenant Governor John A. Lee re
signed from office.

MORE VESSELS
FOR OUR NAVY
House Committee Will Ask
Congress to Provide for
Two Additional Battleships
â
CUT IN APPROPRIATION
Â»
Announcement Is Made That
Sum Originally Requested
for Yards W r ill Be Reduced
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âCongress
will be asked the last week of this month
by the House Committee on Naval
Affairs to authorize the building of
two battleships, two scout cruisers, six
torpedo-boat destroyers and a few
smaller craft of light draught for the
insular service. This statement is
made on authority which cannot be
questioned, and it makes known to-day
for the first time to Just what extent
the economical Intended changes will
be asked to provide money for new
ships for the navy.
It can be stated to-day as a fact not
hitherto known outside of the commit
tee that $5,000,000 was cut out of the
bill prior to the Christmas holidays.
Within a few days at least $3,000,000
more will be excised. The cuts have
been made largely In the navy-yard
section of the appropriation. It Is felt
by Chairman Foss that the navy-yard
can best stand on the waiting list of
money applicants. The amounts al
ready cut out and to be cut out will
reduce the first estimate of the appro
priation to about $109,000,000. Other re
ductions will be made, and the bill as
it will go to the House will call for
about $103,000,000, or $6,000,000 more than
was appropriated last year.
SLAYER OF OLD
MAN IS HANGED
Henry Milton Suffers Death
on Gallows at San Quentin
for Killing James Gilleece
*
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN RAFAEL, Jan. 6.âLarry O'Nell,
alias George Lupton, alias Henry Mil
ton, who murdered James Gilleece, an
aged saloon-keeper, in San Francisco
in 1903, was executed at 10:30 o'clock
this morning at San Quentin Prison.
Milton was remarkably cool as he
mounted the scaffold. He calmly
smoked -a cigarette. Just before the

PROMINENT JUDGE DIES.
John J. Glenn of the Circuit Court In
Illinois Passes Away.
MONMOUTH, 111., Jan. 6.âJohn J.
Glenn, Circuit Court Judge of the
Tenth Illinois District from 1877 to
1903, died to-day. He was 74 years
of age.
Judge Glenn was active in the legal
and political affairs of Illinois for al
most half at century and had an en
viable record on the bench, few of his
decisions having been reversed by the
j Supreme Court. He was a close per
j sonal friend and classmate of White
! law Reid, editor of - the New York
Tribune. His grandfather served as a
member of the company that acted as
General Washington's guard in Vir
ginia, Maryland and the Southern
States during the revolution.

ONE LIVES IN OAKLAND
Former San Francisco 3linis
ter Avers He Was Cleared

Special DUpatch to The Call.
SPOKANE. Jan. 6.âRev. Frederick
Bell, formerly of San Francisco and
now pastor of Humanity's Church, de
clares he will bring suits against three
preachers who he says have been de
faming his character In asserting that
he was convicted of bigamy and is now
living unlawfully with his wife. He
Bays:
"While In Santa Cruz I was arrested
for bigamy, tried and found guilty, but
the court set aside the verdict, believ
ing that there was no intent to violate
the law." Dr. Bell says he fully be
lieved his former wife was dead when
he married his present wife. After
learning that his first wife was alive
he secured a divorce and remarried his
present helpmate.
The preachers accused of defaming
Bell are Rev. E. C. Downey and Rev.
J. A. Crusan of Spokane and Rev. Mr.
Stone of Oakland; Cal. Rev. Mr. Cru
san says:
"All I know of Dr. Bell Is what I
have read in Santa jCruz and San Fran
cisco papers regarding his trial on the
charge of bigamy and what was told
me by the Rev. Mr. Stone, who was
a resident of Santa Cruz at the time.
If the telling of facts which have al"
ready been made public property
through the columns of the press M
criminal libel, then I am guilty."

FRUIT MEN ORGANIZE
Form Association to Find Market for
Dried Products of the
Golden State.
SAN JOSE, Jan. 6.âThe Dried
Fruit Promotion Association, which
will endeavor to find a market for the
dried fruits of this valley, this after
noon effected a permanent organisa
tion. Joseph E>. Radford was elected
president. Colonel Philo Hersey vice
president, and F. H. Babb secretary
treasurer. The president was em
powered to appoint an executive com
mittee of fifteen.
A general discussion of the condi
tions of markets and pfans for selling
the fruit in the East enlivened the
meeting. Paul Shoup, agent of the
Southern Pacific, stated that his road
had abandoned the idea of increasing
the rate on dried fruit East, to pro
vide an advertising fund because of
opposition. The association is .'irmed
of thirty-five members from the lead
ing commercial and fruit organiza
tions of the county.
SAYS CANCER IS NOT
A CONTAGIOUS DISEASE
BOSTON, Jan. 6.âThe Advertiser to
morrow will say that the Harvard
medical commission, which for the past
two years has been making a study of
cancer, will, In Its report to be made
in a few days, declare the malady to be
non-contagious.
The commission will find: First,
cancer is not infectious; second, it is a
hereditary affliction; third, its cause la
as mysterious as that of human life;
fourth, the remedies are either a knife
or a serum.
Dr. E. H. Nichols is at the head of
the commission, which was made pos
sible by a bequest from Mrs. Caroline
B. Crofts, who gave the Harvard medi
cal school $100,000, the Interest of which
Is to be spent on original medical re
search.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1905.
Special DisWh to The Call.
SACRAMENTO. Jan. 6.âOnce more
Speaker Prescott has postponed the
announcement of the Assembly com
! mittees and the members of the lower
house will be kept in suspense a few
days longer regarding who will be on
and who will head the various commit
, tees.
When the Assembly convened this
morning there was much expectation,
as many of the Assemblymen were
in doubt as to how they had finally
been placed and were anxious for the
announcement that was expected.
Speaker Prescott begged the indul
gence of the Assembly and said he
would have to postpone the announce
i ment of committees until Monday
morning, as he had been unable to
solve the nuzzle of fitting more than
one man into any one place.
As a matter of fact, so it is said, the
â  postponement was made by order of
Governor Pardee, who sent word to
Speaker Prescott âand "profane word,"
too, so it was rumored on the floorâ
that he was not yet satisfied and
wished the announcements withheld.
There are many conjectures as to
just what dissatisfies the Governor
with the arrangements of committees
up to date âthe supposition that the
Senatorial fight has something to do
with It of course being given promi
nence, but there are other matters that
are important to the Governor and
these have as much as anything else
to do with his interference.
In the first place the Governor is
very particular about the "Ways and
Means Committee, which has so much
to do with public expenditure and con
sequently the tax rate. It is said he
did not specially favor Stanton for
chairman of this committee, but is
willing he should get the place, pro
viding: the other two members are sat
isfied.
Then there is the new Committee on
Taxation which is of great importance
to the administration, for by it the
proposed new tax legislation, dear to
the Governor's heart, will be consid
ered. Goodrich was selected as chair
man of this committee in the House,
but It was learned that Hahn was to
be chairman of the Senate Committee
on Taxation. Both are from Pasadena,
and it would hardly be proper that any
one city should have the chairmanship
of one committee in both houses. Hahn
will be induced to withdraw, as he does
not care about the place, and Goodrich
will be named in the Assembly.
Assemblyman Moore of San Joaquin
is said to have a special complaint
against the Speaker. He has openly
declared himself for Knight, and it is
said by other anti-Flint Assemblymen
that he is having difficulty in getting
such committee recognition as he de
sires.
There is a story out to the effect that
Senator Muenter and Assemblyman
Beardslee, both of San Joaquin, have
come to Moore's rescue and declared
that if he is not treated with the con
sideration he and they think is due
him they will desert the Flint ranks.
None of the San Joaquin legislators
will make a statement for publication,
but the story of their alleged troubles
comes from colleagues

PRESCOTT'S CHANGE
STIRS SOUTHERNERS
Supporters of Bard Claim
Speaker of the House Was
Sent to Assembly Practi
cally Pledged to Senator
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 6.âThe scene of
the Senatorial contest has shifted from
here to San Francisco. The- exodus of
the men that make laws and Senators
began this morning and continued until
the last train bayward moved out. The
town seems deserted to-night and it
will remain so until at /least Sunday
j evening, when the legislators are ex
pected bark, all properly instructed by
their "constituents" as to how they
are to stand when the vote for United
States Senator is called.
The principal topic of conversation
to-day is Speaker Prescott's announce
ment of fealty to the cause of Frank
P. Flint. While it is true that Pres
cott denies that he came here as a
Bard man, asserting that he left his
home as a free lance with only one
purpose in mmd âthe election of some
man from the south to the United
States Senateâthe supporters of Bard,
though they are not depressed in the
least at what they term Prescott's "un
explained deflection," say the records
will show that he was elected by voters
laboring under the impression that he
would support Bard first and last.
As a basis for their claim they point
to the minutes of the San Bernardino
County convention that nominated
Prescott for the Assembly. Though
there was no anti-Bard sentiment ex
pressed at the convention Senator
Bard's friends, who were in control,
deemed It advisable to indorse the can
didacy of the incumbent for re-election
and as a result the following strong
resolution was unanimously adopted:
BARD IS INDORSED.
We "specially lnJorse and approve of the
work of the Hon. Thomaa R. Bam in the
United State* Senate, where Vie has tiken rank
with the leading: men in that most notable
legislative body on earth, and where he has
proved his ability, his forceful courage and his
character by standing for what he believed to
bÂ« in the Interests of California In the face of
the most powerful opposition that could be ar
rived against him.
We believe that such signal effort on behalf
of the State which he represents should call for
a warm and enthusiastic response Horn the In
terests which he has thus sought to benefit.
Not only so, but his position at the head of the
â¢ 'ommittee on Irrigation, where he will control
the expenditure of millions of dollars in this
and In adjoining and tributary States and Ter
ritories, furnished additional reason why he
whould be continued as one of the representa
tives of California In the Senate at Washing
ton.
We therefore believe that he is the choice of
the Republicans of this county as hlÂ» own
â uccessor.
By virtue, of a nomination placed in
his hands by the convention that pass
.cd this resolution Prescott went before
the voters of his counfcy and was elect
ed. Senator Bard's supporters say there
could be no stronger indorsement of
Senator Bard than that contained in
this resolution and it is claimed by
some of Bard's friends that after Pres
cott's nomination he said he considered
this resolution binding upon him and
that a direct pledge could have been
no more forceful.
HEADQUARTERS DARK.
Speaker Prescott denies that he so
held, however, and it is a question
difficult to determine. Senator Bard's
friends, however, have the last word
in the discussion when they say that
in any the resolution of indorse
ment of Senator Bard's candidacy,
which will admit of no misconstruction
and plainly expresses the desire of the
voters of San Bernardino, has been
rendered purposeless and robbed of all
effect by Speaker Prescott's confession i
that he is intrenched In the camp of
Frank P. Flint. Thus have the de
sires of the men that composed the
San Bernardino convention been de
feated.
The headquarters of the candidates
for the Senatorship were dark to-night,
all appreciating the fact that more
work could be accomplished in San
Francisco than here. There is much
talk of the arrival here upon the trains
with the returning legislators and
avowed candidates of a number of dark
horses, who, to use the expression of
Candidate Arthur Fisk, will be in a
"receptive" mood. These dark horses,
it is pointed out. are looking for a
deadlock In the south and a concen
tration of opposition against the north
ern men that have announced them
selves as in the fight. They must get
in early, however, for California legis
lators are noted for their shyness un
less the dark horse Is full of promises
and is able "to show them why."
The Electoral College of California
will be the center of interest in the
Assembly chamber next Monday after
noon. After the formality of balloting
for President and Vice President of the
United States is concluded the mem- j
bers of the college will select one of I
their number to carry the ballot to
Washington. The impression prevails
here that George H. Pippy will be thus '
honored, but a fight may develop when
the members get together and ambition
is aroused by contemplation of the
dignity that attaches to the office.

RECREANT TO PLEDGE
TO THE SOUTHERNER
"Perfidy*' of the Militiaman
Earns for Him an Excoria- j
tion at Hands of Leading j
Journal of Sunny South j
,
i
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 6.âComment- j
me on the sudden change of front of j
General Prescott, the Los Angeles :
Times will to-morrow say editorially: I
When a Senator or an Assemblyman !
goes to the State Legislature unin- J
structed and unpledged he has the full- j
est freedom of action, and may vote j
for measures or for men according to |
the best (or the worst) dictates of his j
own conscience. But the case is differ
ent when a member of the Legislature j
goes to that body under instructions
or under a pledge to perform a certain
action.
Frank C. Prescott, now Speaker of
the California Assembly, was not spe
cifically instructed to vote for Thomas
R. Bard for United States Senator, but
he was elected with the understanding
that he would support Senator Bard
for re-election to the Senate. His con- |
stituents so understoodâat least the !
majority of them didâand to certain j
persons Mr. Prescott positively pledged
himself to the support of Mr. Bard.
Now Prescott comes out, to the amaze- j
ment of everybody, and announces ;
that he is a supporter of Frank P.
Flint for the Senatorial toga. It would
be interesting to learnâif the inside j
facts were attainableâjust what in- j
fluences have been brought to bear
upon Mr. Prescott to induce him to en
act the role of a turncoat.
His selection for the Speakership was j
a surprise hardly less than that of his !
flop to Flint. While it is not openly j
charged that there was any connection
between these two events, there are
those who profess to find such a con- !
nection as the only plausible explana- j
tion of his singular change of front.
At any rate, It is apparent beyond all
controversy that Speaker Prescott has 1
proved recreant to his pledges in behalf |
of Senator Bard, though in \deserting i
the Bard standard he may have kept i
promises made to others whom It Is not '
necessary to name. His recreancy, it is j
true, is no crime in a legal sense. But i
it is a moral crime and a political of
feme which his constituents whom he
has betrayed will be slow to forget. No i
holder of a public office who is found to
be untrustworthy and Janus-faced is
likely to be given a chance to hold of- ;
flee aft*r his perfidy has been found out. j
The perfidy of Mr. Prescott will give to j
Flint one more vote. It will not ma
terially affect the Senatorial situation.
But it will materially affect the politi- j
cal status of Mr. Prescott. as he will
discover later on.
TRACED SOOTH
FROM THIS CITY
More Light on Movements of
31 rs. Bishop. Who 31 ay Be
Cutler Mountain Victim
Special Difpatch to The Call.
COLORADO SPRINGS. Jan. 6.âln
following up the connection of Mrs.
Augusta I. Bishop's name with the
Mount Cutler tragedy, some circum
stances tending to support the theory
that she was the victim have been un
earthed, although there is some con
fusion in the name. A Mrs. K. E.
Bishop was in Cheyenne Canyon on
November IS in company with a man,
who was evidently a stranger to her.
The couple called at the photograph
studio of J. M. Haves, keeper of the
tollgate. and a picture of the woman
was made by Haves' assistant, J. R.
Miller. Mrs. Bishop wanted her escort
to have his picture taken with heT,
but he refused.
Miller describes Mrs. Bishop as a
woman about five feet three inches in
height, about 125 pounds in weight and
with hair the color of the murdered
woman. She displayed many diamonds
in the studio. She was neatly dressed
and wore a. long coat and a cap. Her
general description tallies with that of
the murdered woman.
Miller is unable to say whether there
was gold in the woman's teeth. The
addrifss left by Mrs. Bishop for the for
warding of the photographs waa the
Palace Hotel, San Francisco. She asked
that they be mailed on November 16. No
word has ever been received from San
Francisco as to whether she ever re
ceived the pictures.
A man and woman, registering as
Mr. and Mrs. K. D. Bishop of Cleve
land, arrived at the Palace Hotel In
this city'on November 18 and departed
a short time later for Los Angeles. The
woman's description, according to the
statement of one of the clerks of the
Palace, corresponded with that con
tained in the above dispatch. The
couple, before leaving, said their des
tination was Los Angeles and they re
quested that any mail that might ar

SENATE FINALLY CONFIRMS
THE NOMINATION OF CRUM
Objection Raised by Tillman to the
Recess Appointment of
the President,
WASHINGTON. Jan. 6.âThe nomi
nation of W. D. Crum, a negro, to be
Collector of Customs for the port of
Charleston, S. C, was confirmed by the
Senate in executive session to-day by a
vote of 33 to 17. Crum had been nomi
nated by the President three times and
had received three recess appointments.
He is now serving under the last of
these recess appointments.
Senator Tillman made a speech de
voted almost entirely to the constitu
tional right of the President to make
a recess appointment when no actual
recess had occurred, the Senate having
adjourned one session and begun
another at noon of the same day.
The race issue played a very insig
nificant part in the debate, which was
participated in by fully a dosen Sen
ators.
When Crum receives his commission
he will be entitled to receive the salary
which has accumulated since his first
appointment.

GREAT DREDGE MINING BOOM
IS COMING TO THE YUBA.
Immense Electric Power
Will Be Made Available.
Shasta County Soon to Have Third Smelter
When the Yuba River is dammed
twenty-two new gold dredgers, already
arranged for, will be operated in the
lower stream. This information is de
rived from W. W. Waggoner, State De
bris Commissioner. A great electric
power has been projected, which can
be utilized in the operation of
this fleet of mechanical gold extract
ors. The Hammon Company is con
structing five dredgers on the Yuba
now, and is working two others. At
least 15,000 horsepower will be gener
ated by damming the narrows of the
Yuba to the height of 150 feet. Wag
goner says it will be possible to dredge
the. present bed of the river from the
Narrows to Daguerre Point. The dam
will cost between $500,000 and $800,000.
The Redding Free Press says that at
French Gulch, Shasta County, 125 men
are employed in the Gladstone mine.
The mill and .mine are running with
electric power. The present total depth
of the mine is 1000 feet.
The third smelter for Shasta County
Is about to be constructed. In antici
pation the Southern Pacific Company
has run a spur track to the smelter site
of the Mammoth Copper Company at
Kennett. The machinery for the smelt
er is being made in the East. Kennett
is growing rapidly.
The Scott Valley Advance reports
that rain and snow have raised the wa
ter hi many mountain streams in Sis
kiyou County sufficiently to permit hy
draulic and placer mines to begin ope
rations. The Cherry Hill mine, which
has been closed on account of litiga
tion, has been reopened and an 1800
--foot tunnel is in construction to tap
the vein at a depth of about 1200 feet.
Mines In the county that have been
closed are preparing to resume. The
mining conditions are consequently im
proved.
MAKING DEVELOPMENTS.
The Santa Fe and Dewey placer
claims at Canada Hill, Placer County,
will be worked, a company having re
cently been formed for that purpose.
Phosphate is reported to have been
discovered in the Grapevine district,
east of Banner, San Diego County, in
paying quantities, occurring in apatite
and other formations in several parallel
ledges. Two thousand acres have been
located by the discoverer.
According to Kern County advices,
H. H. Blood of San Francisco has thir
ty-five men working in the Zada mine
in the Amalie district. Twenty men
are working in the same district in the
Barbarossa mine. J. B. Ferris of Cali
ent* has organized a company to oper
ate the Bright Star mine in the Pinto
district, adjoining the Amalie.
â¢A new smelter will be built by the
Mount Whipple and Copper Basin
mining companies on the Colorado
River, in San Bernardino County, thir
ty miles south of Needles.
A small mining boom is reported in
Vallecito, Calaveras County. A pocket
of gold was recently found in a gulch
through which the people of Calaveras
County had been passing for many
years without entertaining a suspicion
that there was any gold in the imme
diate neighborhood. The pocket yield
ed $500. In the vicinity a large quan
tity of milling quarts has been found
since the pocket came to light and a
small quartz mill has been erected to
crush the rock that has been taken
out. Gravel mining has been done near
the place, but on a small scale. The
Prospect says:
A very â¢mall part of the rich ancient river
channel la being worked by the drifting pro
cess In the Douglas Flat and Vallecito min
ing district, which aids considerable In keep
ing alive the mining industry and Insuring
a small snug fortune for the few who ara
engaged in this class of placer mining. The
owners of the Douglas Flat '40 ancient river
bed placer mine are hoisting pay gravel now.
The Sloan brothers are drifting out bonanza
gravel from their ancient river bed placer
mine in Balaklava Mountain. The old river
bed here ran across a small lime rock range
which, strange to say, drains all the channel
water from the pay gravel and leaves It as
â ry and nice as plowed land, although the
bed of the old river at this place Is nearly
three hundred feet below the apex of the
mountain.
A large project is reported in the
Ouanacevle district in Mexico, Involving
an outlay of $5,000,000. A company has
secured a grant of 9000 acres and will
tunnel 10,000 feet through a mountain
at a distance of 2200 feet below the sur
face to tap veins of gold, silver and
copper. The Guanacevie district is re
ported to be the most thoroughly mined
section in Mexico. It takes In a terri
tory of six miles long and four miles
wide.
LOS ANGELES MINERS.

a mining company has been organized
to operate mining properties In Lower
California, forty miles east of Ensen
ada, In the bed of a river. Two other
Los Angeles companies have been or
ganized to work on properties Just
across the line from Imperial.

UTAH'S BANNER YEAR,
'The Salt Lake Tribune, in a special
number, sums up the result of mining
operations in Utah during the past
year. The output of gold is estimated
at $6,518,036. The silver is valued at
$7,774,979; the copper at $7,221,756; the
lead at $5,020,550; the quicksilver,
amounting to 700 flasks, at $31,500. The
total as summed up in round figures is
$26,537,000. The apparent gain as com
pared with 1903, as figured out by the
Tribune, is $4,522,264. Divldents to the
amount of $4,156,000 were declared. The
Daly West paid $1,034,000 and the Sil
ver King $1,300,000. Thus one-half of
the entire sum paid in dividends must
be credited to two properties. The rec
ord for the year both for the mines and
the furnaces shows an increase In out
put.
The Tribune naturally puts its best
foot forward in its comments on the
mining statistics of the State. In part
it says:
An analysis of the exhibit reveals, an In
crease in the production of each of the metals
excepting that of silver, and In this the dim
inution is confined to less than half a million
dollars. The most striking feature of the con
trast Is that which tells of the astounding
Increase In the production of copper, nor was
it until the last quarter of the year that the
copper producers were permitted to assert the
tonnages of which their mines are capable.
The Increase in the crop of rold, notwith
standing the decadence of some of the camps
that were formerly most productive of that
metal. Is a well-defined one, while the output
of lead overwhelmed the most liberal predic
tion and exceeded all previous records.
The story of the present year is easily fore
told. Not a record established In 1904 but will
succumb to that which shall follow its close.
The most conspicuous gain will undoubtedly
be in the output of copper, and copper Is king
To that end the sinews have been provided at
the mills and furnaces; nature has supplied the
balance. That the American has ordered an
enlargement of Its plant In the valley and
that the United States Smelting Company has
consecrated a new one to the lead producer
anticipates an Increase In the output of that
metal, while that silver and gold will reveal
an increase Is foretold In the regeneration of
old camps in which those metals have always
been most prominent. Never, Indeed, has a
year of greater promise dawned upon the
camps of a commonwealth.
In the mines as well as at the furnaces
and the mills an unprecedented volume of
employment has been afforded the wage
earner, with new sources being hourly added
to those already opened to him, while the
condition throughout Is one over which every
Interest is permitted to exult as never before
In the Industrial history of the State.
CITY HALL DESTROYED
AT SPRINGFIELD, MASS.
Building Falls Prey to Flames and
Many People Barely Escape
From Structure.
SPRINGFIELD, Mass., Jan. 6.âThe
City Hall, valued at more than

fire to-day. There were probably fifty
people in the building and there were

safely. The police station and court
room across the street took fire sev
eral times, but were only slightly dam-
aged.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY. JANUARY 7. 1905.
SHARP WORDS
IN THE HOUSE
Littauer and Baker Have a
Tiff During* Discussion of
the Fortifications Bill
MEASURE IS PASSED
Question of Eemission of
Duties on War Materials
Starts a Brisk Colloquy
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âThe House
to-day passed the fortifications bill.
After Congressman Littauer of New
York had drawn a lesson from the
siege of Port Arthur to show that sub
marine mines had proved of the great
est importance in warfare, Congress
man Baker enlivened the proceedings
by attacking the President, who, he
said, wanted to hold himself up as the
"counterpart of Wilhelm 11, the great
war god."
When the concluding paragraph of
the bill was reached, relating to the re
mission of duties on such war mate
rials as might be purchased abroad,
Baker offered an amendment striking
out that feature of the bill and in
quired of Littauer if that had been in
corporated at the request of the Secre
tary of War.
"Yes," said Littauer, "the present and
all former Secretaries."
Baker then charged that Secretary
Taft was a member of an administra
tion which advanced the proposition
that the foreigner pays the tax.
"Now, why." he Inquired, "are you
going to make this present to the for
eigner?"
Littauer responded that It was net
the time for that question to be dis
cussed.
Baker thereupon exclaimed: "Oh,
yes, it is always the time, when you
people are shown to be frauds in your
contention that the foreigner pays the
tax. That is always appropriate."
The bill was passed without amend
ment.
On motion of Grosvenor of Ohio th*
committee of the whole House was
discharged from further consideration
of the bill to revise the laws relating
to the regulation of nteam vessels and
the bill was referred back to the Com
mittee on Merchant Marine and Fish
eries.
â  The House then adjourned until Mon
day.
BACON ON THE GRILL.
Chairman of Interstate Commerce
Board Before House Committee.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 6.âThe meeting
of the House Committee on Interstate
Commerce to-day was replete with sen
sational Incidents. A hearing was given
to persons interested in the Cooper-
Quarles bill. E. P. Bacon of Milwau
kee, chairman of the Interstate Com
merce Commission, was first heard.
From the moment he took the stand
until he left it Bacon was subjected to
scathing.rebuke by Mann of Illinois
for certain statements made by him in
a publication known as Freight, as well
as in public addresses. Mann quoted
from a statement alleged to have been
made by Bacon that owing to the op
position of the majority of the two com
mittees of Congress there was no pos
sibility of legislation to further restrict
the carriers.
"Do you think that was true?" in
quired Mann.
Bacon answered that he believed It
was true at the time.
"I know," angrily replied Mann, "that
was a libel and slander. I know It was
false and I believe you knew it was
false."
Bacon calmly replied that he did not
want to be classed as a falsifier, which
only served to further anger Mann, who
.said that people who write articles
must be held responsible for what they
write.
Again reading from the publications
referred to, Mann said it was stated in
Bacon's address before the St. Louis
convention "that over three-fourths of
the Representatives in Congress owed
their presence there to the influence of
the railroads," and he asked Bacon if
that statement was his. Bacon an
swered that his remarks had been ex
temporaneous, but he had no recollec
tion of making the statement.
"I believe," said Mann, "you thought
any man in the country could go out
and libel any member of Congress. It
fortunately is true that people gener
ally pay no attention to such state
ments."
Bacon insisted he never made the
statement, but admitted saying that
four members of the House committee
had declared they would permit no ac
tion on the part of the committee on
any bill until definite action was taken
upon the Cooper-Quarles bill.
"We want to ascertain who these
four men are," demanded Mann. The
witness, however, refused to answer,
saying it would not be proper for him
to name them.
Shackleford of Missouri then indig
nantly inquired, "I ask you, sir, if Mr.
Shackleford was one of them?"
"No, sir," was the reply.
"You went over to the Elkins bill,"
said Mann, "and because the commit
tee won't follow you fast enough you
say we are under the influence of the
railways."
Bacon denied making the statement
and added that he had kept back cer
tain elements and interests in regard
to the proposed legislation.
The witness admitted that after the
revised Elkins bill was agreed upon he
had endeavored to have it become a
law.
Mann then spoke of a banquet given
at the Union League Club In Chicago
at which he said an attempt was made
to Influence him in favor of the Elkins
bill.
"I laughed at them," said. Manr..
"When they read the pooling clause of
the bill I said it had as much chance
as a snowfiake in Hades."

OUR FORESTRY
POLICY SCORED
George Maxwell, a Delegate
From California, Belabors
Congress for Inaction
LOSS IN THE MILLIONS
He Would Spend as Much on
Nation's Timber Interests
as on Forts and Navies
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âThe Impor
tance of public forest lands to mining
was the theme of the morning meeting
of the concluding session of the Amer
ican Forest Congress. A L. Fellows,
consulting engineer of the Government
reclamation service, discussed the de
velopment of water power as related to
forest reserves. He contended that the
forest areas should be reserved by the
Government and should not be left
In the unregulated domain of the
United States.
Dr. David T. Day, chief of the di
vision of mineral resources of the
United States Geological Survey, con
tended that it was no longer necessary
to give coal, asphalt and other mineral
lands In the Government domain praN
tically free to prospectors, and said
that the Government should apply some
such regulations to govern the sales
and leases of these public mineral
lands as are applied to the lands be
longing to Indians.
MAXWELL TAKES FLOOR.
A sensational address was made by
George H. Maxwell, the executive
chairman of the National Irrigation
Congress, who spoke as a delegate from
California. He scored Congress for its
refusal to repeal the timber and stone
act and declared that the House Public
Lands Committee, the Western mem
bers of which he alleged had "delib
erately prevented action" on the meas
ure, and thus "allowed the stealing to
continue, should be held up to public
obloquy and lashed at the cart's tall
with the whip of outraged national
sentiment."
He took Issue with a statement in
President Roosevelt's address of yes
terday by saying that If the forests
were left to the West they would con
tinued to be "skinned," but added, "It
Is to Eastern sentiment and such men
as Theodore Roosevelt and Grover
Cleveland that we have forest reserves
and protection."
Continuing Maxwell said:
Last year, with 156,000 acres in the Chip
pewa Indian reservation, Minnesota, the
stumpage alone was sold, and the Government
realized Sls 06 an acre, still owning the land.
At this rate the Government has lost under
Its disposal of 3,000,000 acres through the Um
ber and atone act slnoo President Roosevelt's
message in December, 1902, recommending its
repeal, 938,578,000. besides losing control of
tbe land, but the majestic timber of Oregon
and the far Northwest, where the timber
stealing is now centered, la worth double that
of Minnesota, and in these two year* the Gov
ernment has lost 978,000,000, or 93.000.009 a
month.
CHANGES SUGGESTED.
Maxwell made the following recom
mendations:
Create immediately Into forest reserves every
acre of public forest lands more valuable for
timber than mining or agriculture; pass the
bill consolidating all Government forest work
in the Department of Agriculture, as re com- i
mended by the President; repeal the Umber ,
and stone act. as recommended by the Presl
dent, and thus stop gigantic timber steals; |
pass the Appalachian bill; plant trees on the
great plains; appropriate as much for forest !
planting as for forts and navies, and repeal ,
all tariff upon wood, lumber, pulp and forest
products.
Captain Beth Bullock, supervisor of
the Black Hills Forest Reserve, dis
cussed the question "Will the Admin
istration of the Forest Reserves on a
Conservative Basis Retard Mining?"
He pointed out "how in the successful
prosecution of the work required to
make a mine productive and remunera
tire to Its owners the use of forest tim- i
ber is an absolute necessity. It could
be truly said that a veritable forest
has been used under ground in the
mines of the Black Hills during the â¢
few years they have been in operation.
The committee on resolutions pre
sented its report to-day. It urges,
among ether things, full protection and
preservation of the forests; repeal of
the timber and stone act and the en
actment of a substitute authorizing
the proper Federal officers to sell tim
ber growing on public lands when the
sale shall be for the public welfare;
the planting and preservation of shade
trees along the public highways j
throughout America; increase of op- j
portunities for general forest education i
In educational institutions; immediate j
purchase of the Calaveras grove of big
trees by the National Government and
reconveyance of the Tosemlte Valley
to the National Government.

TO MEET WITH
THE PRESIDENT
Congressmen Invited to the
White House to Talk Over
Legislation for the Session
TO PROMOTE HARMONY
JSeonomy, Control of Trusts
and the Tariff Will Be
Discussed at Conference
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âPresident
Roosevelt has invited a number of Re
publican Senators and Representatives
to a conference at the White House for
to-morrow afternoon. It is understood
that the object is to consider the con
dition of the Treasury, the situation as
to appropriations and the necessity for

ference to harmonize differences, which
have arisen among the Republicans as
to the nature of the legislation to be
enacted at the present session, and to
secure, if possible, unanimity of action
regarding legislative action in the fu
ture. It is indicated that the confer
ence may take a wide scope.
An effort is being made in Congress
by Speaker Cannon and the members of
the Senate Committee on Appropria
tions to scale down all appropriations
to be passed at the present session.
This has aroused some antagonism
among Senators and Representatives,
who are Interested in appropriations
for river and harbor improvements, for
public buildings and for the extension
of the navy. It is expected that these
differences will be threshed out at the
conference to be held to-morrow.
It is suggested that legislation re
garding the proposed increase of the
powers of the Interstate Commerce
Commission, the tariff and the Federal
incorporation of companies doing an
interstate business will be considered.
It is known that the President Is in
terested in the subject of the tariff to
such an extent that he may call an ex
traordinary session of Congress for its
consideration. It is said to be quite
Improbable that a session earlier than
next fall will be called, and it is not
certain that one will be called for that
time. The President desires, however,
that action be taken by Congress, both
as to the tariff and as to the increase
of the powers of the Interstate Com
merce Commission, and he hopes this
action may be taken at a reasonably
early date. Members of Congress, who
are frequent callers at the White
House, have indicated that no action
on the tariff is likely to take place be
fore next fall.

WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âThe Senate
Committee on Territories to-day au
thorized a favorable report on the
nomination of John G. Brady to be
Governor of Alaska. The nomination
has been held up several weeks at the
request of Pennsylvania commercial
interests in Alaska.

MAY COMMUNE
WITH THE DEAD
; Dr. Heber Newton Believes
the Living Are in Touch
With Spirits of Departed
MESMERISM A REALITY
â¦
Asserts His Faith in What
| He Says Has Been Regarded
Generally as Superstition;
NEW YORK, Jan. 6.âDiscussing
psychical science in an address delivered
i yesterday, at a meeting of the American
j Institute for Scientific Research, the
i Rev. Dr. R. Heber Newton, formerly
\ pastor at Stanford University, made ths
' assertion that the spirits of the dead I
! communicate with the living, that telep
j athy is a power possessed by many
' men and women, that clairvoyance is
!an .established fact of science, that it
I may be possible for men to carry halos
about their heads and, In short, that a
variety of beliefs regarded by the ma
jority of persons as superstitions can
be demonstrated as truths. He said in
part:
Mesmerism was laughed out of court at tha
opening of our century, and It is back again.
In good standing, under the alias of hynotlsm.
So one may run on through a list of strange,
unaccountable, mysterious and most unbeliev
able powers of man, leading up to that night
mare of the dogmatic scientist, spiritualism.
For the first time in the history of man these
powers have been scientifically investigated in
our day. Already the result is that a consid
erable number of eminent men of science have
had the courage to avow that-, after allowing
for Illusion, fraud and every possible hypoth
esis of interpretation, and they have been driven
to the ultimate solution of the problemâthe
belief In the actual communication of the
spirits of those whom we call dead with the
living.
The possibilities of mental medicines are
only being opened. Its application to the most
distressing form of human malady, insanity, la
full of beneficent results. Its potenoy in char
acter reform and the cure of the drink habit
seems vast and benign.
Religious faith is finding it* true foundations
In the recognition of man as a spiritual being,
a being who has had dominion over nature
given to him. as the child of a vaster spiritual
being, tho Lord of all life.
MORE FOOD EXPERIMENTS
WITH THE POISON SQUAD
Effect of Preservatives, Coloring Mat
ter and Cold Storage Will
Be Studied.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 6.âThe so
called "poison squad." consisting of
clerks of the Department of Agricul
ture, who have volunteered for ser
vice in carrying out the food preserva
tive and coloring matter experiments
ot Chief Chemist Wiley, will reassem
ble next Monday and continue the ex
periments until March. The effects of
artificial coloring of food on the sys
tem will be studied. They will then
have a respite until the middle of
April, when the work will be resumed
until July L
While continuance of these expert- â 
roents beyond that date has not been
finally determined upon, it is prob
able that during next year a similar
squad will be utilized under the same
strict rules to carry on the experiments
as to cold storage food products. ,
These tests will have in view the de
termining of the exact effect of cold j
storage on foods and the rapidity of
the development of bacteria under
cold storage conditions.

ON NEW YORK ELEVATED
Ono Man Killed and Half a Dozen
Persons Injured During
Rush Honrs.
NEW YORK. Jan. 6.âOne man waa
killed and half a dozen persons were
seriously Injured In a rear-end col
lision in which three trains crashed
together on the elevated railroad at
Horatio and Greenwich streets during
the rush hours early to-day. The man
who was killed was a railroad work- j
man, who was repairing a truck on '
a broken down train. The injured
were passengers.
The accident is said to have been
due to the failure to flag the third
train.

NAN PATTERSON
IS DENIED RAIL
Court Rules That She Must
Remain in Jail Pending
Trial on Mnrder Charge
REFUSES CONSOLATION
Prisoner Breaks Down When
Informed of the Decision
and Becomes Hysterical
NEW YORK. Jan. 6.âJustice Green
bauio of the New York State Supreme
Court to-day denied the application of
Nan Patterson for bail pending a new
trial on the charge of having murdered
"Caesar" Young.
Miss Patterson became hysterical
when Abraham Levy, her counsel, went
to the Tombs and informed her of
Justice Greenbaum's decision. She wept
and waved her arms frantically, re
fusing to be comforted.
Later, when her father visited her.
an affecting scene took place. The
Xoung woman tried to restrain her
emotion, but finally threw her arms
about her father's neck and burst Into
tears again. He, too, was overcome
and suddenly collapsed, and it was
necessary to send him home In a cab.

BROSBLASTS ANNOY
BADEN PEOPLE
Say They Feel Earth Shake
When Railroad Laborers
Explode Tons of Dynamite
HILLS ARE BLOWN AWAY
Detonations Cause Residents
Much Inconvenience and
They Theorize on the Case
The residents of Baden, Ocean "View.
- Colma and adjoining towns are much
wrought up over the heavy blasting,
' that is being done by the Southern Pa- j
cine Company In the construction of its
new cut-off about five miles from Ba
% den. The citizens firmly believe that
the blasts are responsible for many of
.'â¢the numerous shake-ups felt recently.
â¢ For nearly two months blasts have i
been going off night and day at short
'. intervals. Some of them are small and
explode without annoying, while others
are as heavy as it is possible to make
them, and when they explode the clti- j
sens cf the adjoining districts say tho
.whole country for miles around quivers
.and quakes just like an earthquake.
The officials of the company and the j
contractors who are working on the j
' new road deny that the blasts are felt :
â¢in the vicinity. They admit that some j
heavy charges of dynamite have been;
â¢et off since the work began, but add ;
that the explosions were never felt and
' â¢ that it would be impossible for the
. shock to effect the district miles away.
The last heavy blast was set off on
Hew Year's day. Late in the afternoon
â¢ thirty tons of dynamite were exploded, I
and the entire side of a huge, rocky hill I
â¢ fully 300 feet long was blown away and
Its fragments were scattered far and
near. A saloon man In the vicinity says
his beer kegs were hurled from their
positions and his saloon was nearly
wracked. The people of Baden were
terrified when they felt the earth shak
ing.
Blasting has been going on steadily I
at two points along the old road that
leads froih Baden to South San Fran
cisco. The earth has been loosened all j
â¢along the line and two mammoth steam
' .shovels are at work clearing away the
â¢ rock and dirt that has become loosened
by the blasts. Work on the new tunnel
'. â¢ near South San Francisco will begin In
a few days, and it is probable that
some more heavy blasting will be done
; â¢ there.
' Strange to say, the residents who are \
â¢complaining about the blasts never;
hear a report when they feel the shook.
.Ail the reports have been smothered,
for the reason that the dynamite works
down and the rock and clay are loos
ened from beneath. The heaviest of j
' ', these blasts have torn away entire sec- j
.. tions of ground, while the work aceom
. plished by the light blasts is hardly no- :
/ â  ticeable.
The people who reside in Belmont and
â¢ . . other towns farther down say they
- ; riever experienced any shocks when the j
..Baden folks complained of quaking
I . earth.' Experts say the rock and clay
. . run in veins, and that Invariably the
' grain points north rather than south,
and for this reason the supposed shock
â¢â : would travel in the direction of the
grain.

cate with.the legislative bodies now in
