ANNUAL SALE, TEN MILLION BOXES
BYINGTON SAYS
HALT TO JURY
Several Indictments Found
in Eppinger Cases and
Attorney Is Overwhelmed
IMPOSE TOO MUCH WORK
Inquisitors Will Be Called
to Stand at Arraignment
of Chan Cheung To-Day
The Grand Jurors are blasting the
mountain of fraud in the Eppinger
grain cases, and if their present pur
poses are carried out there will be an
upheaval that will spread quakiness
far and wide. Witnesses who are al
leged to have committed perjury at the
trial of Jacob Eppinger some months
ago, in the effort to save him from
conviction, are particularly the aim of
the Grand Jury. Nor is any one im
plicated in the gigantic scheme of
fraudulent Issues of warehouse certifi
cates to be allowed to escape.
Several indictments were found in
the ca3e last night, but District Attor
ney Byington insisted on having sev
eral days to prepare the charges for
formal presentation. He complained
that the Grand Jury was about over
whelming him with its demands for
indictments.
Foreman Andrews was asked wheth
er Jacob Eppinger's sons were the sub
jects of inquiry, but he would neither
deny nor affirm. Nothing has been
done toward preferring charges against
Bernard Ettlinger, as has been erron
eously reported.
The Grand Jury will appear before
Judge Lawlor this morning with three
indictments in minor cases. Louis Ep
stein, a Mission street second-hand
dealer, will be accused of receiving
stolen property. According to the evi
dence submitted Epstein was in league
with a clerk in the store of the Pacific
Hardware and Steel company. The
latter stole from the stocks and Ep
stein sold the booty.
At 11 o'clock the Jurors will be called
as witnesses at the arraignment of
Chan Cheung before Judge Lawlor. The
attorneys for Chan will attempt to
show that In finding the indictment
against him for the murder of Tom
Tick the Jurors were actuated by un
just and forbidden motives. It will be
contended that the true bill for mur
der was returned against the Chinese
because he would not tell of the police
corruption fund in Chinatown, which
he Is believed to have handled. The
methods of "sweating" Chan, re
sorted to by the police committee of
the Grand Jury, are to have full air
ing, declare his counsel, and the Grand
Jurors expect a warm grilling on the
witness stand.
FORMER RUSSIAN OFFICER
MAKES SOME REVELATIONS
rells of Cmel ty and Method of Oast
tag the Innocent Into Exile
In Siberia.
"Political Banishment to Saghalien
Siberia," was the subject of a power
fully dramatic recitation of cruelty
brutality, cunning, fraud and false
hood presented before the members of
the British and American Union las
night by Lieutenant Colonel Alexand
er Lochwltzky, formerly of the Dra
goon Guards, one of Russia's crack
regiments.
The colonel told of how he, Ilk
many another, suffered degradatio
and exile because it was charged
without proof, without even inquiry o
giving him a chance to face the
charge, that he was a member of the
reform society. He declared that he
was asked to divulge the names ol
'his accomplices," and though he de
clared he had none and was not a
member of any such society his feet
were chained and with gangs of the
worst criminals, as well as many In
nocent persons, he was banished to
Siberia. The story of his escape waa
thrilling. Colonel Lochwitzky waa
heartily applauded throughout his
iecture.
Dr. Frederick W. d'Evelyn spoke on
'The Congo," and having a close ac
juaintance with that part of Africa
md its people was able to give a vivid
picture of cruelties practiced by the
jfficials of King Leopold.

STILL IN JAIL
31 rs. Dorothy McCade's Case
Puzzles the Police, but
She Declares Innocence
HER 3IOTHER IS FOUND
Accused Woman Says She
Will Be Cleared of Charges
Brought Against Her
The ledger account of Mrs. Dorothy
McCade, whose penchant for offering
checks on banks in which she had over
drawn her credit, *is still puzzling the
police. Mrs. McCade, or McDade, as
she spells it on her trunks, has added
two additional debts to her column of
"I. O. U's," and the detectives are at a
loss to know whether the woman has
been acting in good faith or has run
the gamut of human weakness with
the idea of playing upon it.
The account at present stands:
DebtorâSeveral drafts on banks of
Southern California, a few unpaid ho
tel bills and a deal of explanation due.
CreditâSome good clothes, a woman's
ways, a somewhat pretty face
and a bankbook on a San Bernardino
bank that shows a balance due of
something over $300, but no punctured
slips to show what has been drawn
against it.
Technically Mrs. "McCade," as per
card, and Mrs. "McDade," as per
trunks, is not a criminal. She has not
done anything that makes her-answer
able to the law unless the charge be
that of obtaining goods under false
pretenses. Her bankbook is with the
Farmers' Bank of San Bernardino and
that institution reports that the
woman's husband, or a man represent
ing himself as such, had deposited an
amount against which she was sup
posed to draw. However, on a recent
date the sum was withdrawn by the
man, and it is possible that the woman
did not know this.
Mrs. McCade, however, has led a
mysterious life. When she registered
at the St. Francis Hotel she did so at a
time when the Maxine Elliott Company
had signed its names to the register.
This inspired the idea of becoming an
actress, and upon the theory that the
stage would afford her a remunerative
living It Is supposed that Mrs. McCade
acted.
When Mrs. McCade registered at the
St. Francis there was stopping at the
hotel one J. B. Rice, who is a promi
nent traveling man and a representa
tive of an Eastern powder company.
Mr. Rice and Mrs. McCade were good
friends and he would perhaps be able
to tell much of the young woman's life
if he were here, but he has left town.
The police yesterday learned that
checks signed in the writing of Mrs.
McCade In favor of the firm of Wein
stock & Lubin and the Emporium Com
pany had been cashed. They were given
in exchange for goods some days ago
and drawn on a San Bernardino bank.
When questioned, Mrs. McCade denied
having issued any checks other than
that payable to R. D. Davis & Co.
Yesterday she was visited several times
by a young man named Farren, who
claims to be acting as her attorney
and who knew the woman in Southern
California.
Mrs. McCade was a Miss Dorothy Al
bright. Her mother lives at Fenner,
Cal., and she has a sister-in-law, a
Mrs. Ida McCade, who has been living
at the Hotel Eugene on O'Farrell street
in this city. A. L. *Shinn, an attorney
of Los Angeles, is acquainted with Mrs.
McCade. The woman sent him a pres
ent of a pretty piece of silverware, but
for some reason best known to him
self the lawyer returned the gift. She
was also a friend of Henry Overhalt of
Los Angeles and appears to have been
known widely among men of promi
nence In* Southern California.
"I will be cleared of this," said Mrs.
McCade yesterday afternoon. "I am not
criminally liable for the checks I have
Issued, as they were tendered in good
faith. All will be settled and I will be
cleared of these silly charges."
No charge has been entered against
Mrs. McCade and it is thought that she
will be released from custody to-day
unless some one shows a disposition to
prosecute. The check she offered the
St. Francis Hotel was not in payment
of her bill, but merely for collection.
TRIAL OF CHARLES WYMAN
MAKES LITTLE PROGRESS
District Attorney Expects to Finish
Offering Testimony for Prosecu
tion at Next Session.
Those who attended the Wyman
trial yesterday in expectation of a sen
sation were disappointed. Nearly the
entire morning: was taken up by the
reading of *the primary election notice
offered as evidence by the defense.
In the afternoon Registrar Adams was
called to the stand to verify the pro
ceedings of the election. Attorney
Countryman fought every scrap of evi
dence ofTered.
At one time the accused ballot-box
stuffer's attorney strayed from the
track to an address on the privilege of
cross-questioning. The affidavit of
registration, signed by Wyman, was
verified by Deputy Registrar J. X.
Strand and was entered as an exemp
lar of the accused's handwriting. Fair
fax Wheelan was present in the court
room in the afternoon. The trial will
be resumed at 9:30 o'clock on Mon
day morning. At that time, District
Attorney Byington says, the testimony
for the prosecution will be completed.

SURGEON'S COOL SKILL
SAVES CHILD'S LIFE
Dr. E. W. Stevens of the Harbor Hos
pital Performs a Daring and
Successful Operation.
The carelessness of a druggist in the
Popular Drug Store at Broadway and
Dupont street yesterday Jeopardized
the life of little Wong You, who lives
at 629 Pacific street, and the Chinese
child's life was saved only by a dar
ing and unusual operation performed
by Harbor Hospital Surgeon W. E.
Stevens, assisted by Dr. Glover.
Wong You, the son of Wong Look,
who conducts a restaurant at 642
Jackson street, was taken by his
mother to the drug store. She asked
the attendant for a dose of castor oil
fcr the child and received a quantity
of carbolic acid.
Little suspecting the mistake the
child gurped down the flecy liquid and
fell writhing to the floor in convul
sions. An ambulance soon arrived and
little Wong You was taken to the
Harbor Hospital. There Dr. Stevens
discovered that the trachea and wind
pipe had become congested and closed,
so that the child was choking to
death.
The surgeon resolved upon heroic
measures. With the aid of Dr. Glover
the patient was placed on the table,
the sufferer's throat and trachea were
slit open with a keen knife and a sil
ver tube was inserted. An antidote to
the poison was administered and the
child was able to breathe and will live.

TAILOR AND HIS WIFE
MEND THEIR TROUBLES
Mrs. Week's Suit for Divorce and His
Cross-Complaint Are Dismissed
in Court. Â»
The divorce suit of Lillian P. Week
against John R. Week, a tailor, in
which he was charged with cruelty,
was dismissed in Judge Seawell's court
yesterday, indicating a reconciliation.
She based her accusations on his habit
of staying out until after midnight and
the fact hat. he once struck her in the
face. He filed a cross-complaint de
claring that she was cruel to him in
that she sent their baby to live with
relatives because it was troublesome.
Annie Thompson yesterday siled
Frank Thompson for divorce. He is a
steward, but owns a saloon at 30 East
street, and recently sued her to re
cover his community interest in $2000
on deposit in bank. In her complaint
she says that they have lived apart
for the greater portion of their mar
ried life of eleven years and that the
money in bank is her separate prop
erty.
A suit for separate maintenance was
begun by May Richards against Dave
E. Richards, a Southern Pacific fire
man. They were married in August,
1903, and he deserted her a month ago.
Suits for divorce were begun by Jeffer
son C. Brooks against Ada M. Brooks,
desertion; Selma de Silva against
Aloysis Jose de Silva, neglect.
Judge Troutt ordered Albert M.
Weber to pay $20 within ten days for
the support of his wife and two babies
or go to jail. Weber is a master
painter and his wife, on account of re
ligious belief, will not sue him for di
vorce on account of neglect, but has
asked the court to compel him to sup
port her.
SUNSET DISTRICT LEADS IN
THE VAN OF IMPROVEMENTS
Many Streets Opened and Inducements
Offered to Seekers of Homesteads
for Permanent Residence.
According to Secretary Edward
Ewald of the Sunset District Improve
ment Club, as shown by his report,
just compiled, the valley that a few
years ago was covered with sand is
now an ideal place for homes. Streets
and avenues have been opened all the
way from First avenue on the east to
Ninetenth avenue on the west and
from H street on the north to M street
on the south. Thousands of tons of
sand have been carted off this space and
streets and avenues laid out, paved
and sewered, ready for building.
It is the intention of the residents
of this district to have Nineteenth ave
nue graded and paved from H street
to its junction with Dewey boulevard.
The connection with Ocean avenue and
Ingleside roadway will make a pic
turesque driveway from the Presidio,
leading through Golden Gate Park out
and into SanfMateo County.
By the co-operation of the City Sur
veyor and at the expense of the prop-
erty-owners a system of sewerage has
been established, which drains the en
tire section into one mammoth sewer at
Fifteenth avenue. This runs through
Golden Gate Park, connecting with the
big sewer at D and Fulton streets, on
the north of Uie park, where It is car
ried into the bay on the north side
of Richmond.
Other less offensive refuse is car
ried into a septic tank within Golden
Gate Park where it is used as a fer
tilizer for portions of the grounds yet
needing soil.
Clerfayt is Knighted.
Jules Clerfayt, general Western
agent of the International Sleeping
Car Company and the Trans-Siberian
Railway, has been advised of the feet
that in consideration of his services in
piloting visiting Persians through this
country, he has been made a knight
of "The Lion of the Sun." This mark
of distinction cost the young transpor
tation agent considerable trouble and
annoyance recently while he was
guiding one of Persia's distinguished
but bankrupt noblemen across the
country. The latter frequently called
on Clerfayt to meet his Highness' bills
on promises to settle with him when
he returned to Persia. It is believed
that the knighthood is one means the
nobleman has resorted to to quiet
Clerfayt's claims for remuneration.
PAUL RUBENS'
LATEST PLAY
"Lady Madcap,". Exploiting
Pranks of Peer's Daughter,
an Attractive Stage Piece
LONDON, Jan. â"Lady Madcap,"
the new musical piece by Paul Rubens
and Newnham Davis, which George
Edwardes produced last Saturday
night, has been waited for with un
usual Interest. Paul Rubens, who sup
plies the music and some of the words
of the new play, is the author of
"Three Little Maids," and in his latest
effort several of the company who
played In the "Little Maids" in the
United States, and more recently In
Australia, make their reappearance.
Chief of these is G. P. Huntley, whose
acting as a silly swell has made a
big hit.
Curiosity was rife to see whether
these players had been supplied with
another vehicle worthy of their tal
ents.
Although Paul Rubens has not writ
ten another "Three "Little Maids," the
play which he and Newnham Davis
have put together is an attractive one
as It stands, and promises to be an un
commonly attractive one by the time
it Is presented to American audiences.
Newnham Davis is a retired lieutenant
colonel In the army, who has written
amusingly for several years about
gastronomic matters the world over,
and who now makes his first appear
ance as a librettist.
IN LOVE WITH SOLDIER.
"Lady Madcap" is all about the pranks
of Betty, a peer's pretty daughter. She
has fallen in love with a young soldier
named Smith, as the result of having
seen him make a "century" at cricket.
Hearing that his regiment is stationed
close by, she invites the whole outfit
to her father's country seat, Egbert
Castle. Now, Lord Framlingham de
tests soldiers, so Lady Betty sends him
a telegram, purporting to come from
the Foreign Office, as the result of
which he posts off to London, taking
with him his highly severe butler.
When the soldiers arrive, however,
Lady Betty discovers that her hero,
Smith, is only a private, and therefore
unable to meet her on equal terms. As
a matter of fact, he is Auroya Brown,
a young millionaire, who is doing a
short tour of soldiering for the fun of
it. But his pretty hostess, knowing
nothing of this, urges him to gain ac
cess to the drawing-room by taking the
place of the absent butler. Smith
agrees, and in order to keep him in
countenance Lady Betty masquerades
as a lady's maid, first persuading her
girl churn, Gwenny Holden, to pass
herself off as the daughter of the house.
Of course, all sorts of complications
arise immediately. The amateur but
ler gets himself into hot water by un
due familiarity with the guests, while
Lady Betty's chum in her assumed
character of a peer's daughter is over
whelmed with attentions, notably from
a French Count (Maurice Farkoa),
who is among the company.
TRIUMPH OF CUPID.
Finally, it having leaked out that the
real hostess is disguised as a lady's
maid, every pretty domestic in the
place is courted in turn in the hope
that she may be Lady Betty.
The end comes when Lord Farmllng
ham returns and bursts in wrathfully
on the merry scene. He is placated,
however, upon discovering that the
young man whom he sees in butler's
dress is a millionaire who has proposed
marriage to his daughter, and all ends
happily.
G. P. Huntley is the make-believe
butler, and when he has worked up his
part more will be quite as amusing as
he was in "Three Little Maids." Madge
Crichton is not in the cast, the part of
Lady Betty being taken by Adrienne
Augarde, another of George Edwardes'
finds and a most winning young wom
an. Delia Mason plays the part .of
Gwenny Holden, Lady Betty's chum,
while George Carroll is a diminutive
corporal.
At present there are not as many good
songs in "Lady Madcap" as there were
in "Three Little Maids," but that is a
matter that can be easily remedied.
Maurice Farkoa, however, has a ditty
called "I Like You in Satin; I Love You
in PluEh," that is sure to be liked on
both sides of the water.
HOLIDAY SPECTACLE.
If holiday plays for children were as
popular in the United States as they
are in this country Arthur Bourchier's
Christmas offering for the young folk
would be sure cf an American produc
tion, for it is all about the famous lit
tle characters in the "Dumpy Books."
Rutland Barrington, the former "Pooh
Bah" of the Savoy company, who
dramatized Kingsley's "Water Babies"
so successfully a year ago, is the au
thor of the new piece. It is called
"Little Black Sambo and Little White
Barbara," and they, not to mention the
two aunts, Dr. Funnyman, Plantagenet.
Black Jumbo and the "tigers." are all
in it as large as life. With tuneful mu
sic, it makes an attractive entertain
ment, which is played by a capable
company, Including Frank Lawton, thÂ«Â»
American whistler of "The Belle of
New York."
DR. DAVID STARR JORDAN
WILL SPEAK AT TRINITY.
"Marriage. Its Sacredness and Duties,**
and "Evils of Divorce" to
Be Discussed.
Dr. David Starr Jordan, president of
Stanford University; the Rev. D.
Charles Gardner, chaplain of the Me
morial Chapel at the university, and
Attorney John A. Wright will speak
at Trinity Church, Bush and Gough
streets, to-morroy night.
"Marriage. Its Sacredness and Du
ties," and "The Evils of Divorce" are
the subjects to be presented. "The
Christian Obligation of Marriage" will
be treated by the Rev. Mr. Gardner.
"The Social Side" will be dealt with
by Dr. Jordan and Mr. Wright will
speak to the proposition that legisla
tion must proceed from the sentiment
of the people.
Trinity's full choir, under the direc
tion of Louis H. Ea/on, organist and
choirmaster, will render the sacred
music.
The watchmaker may pay his bills,
but he lives on tick.

NEWS OF THE COUNTY OF ALAMEDA
SWINDLER USES
FORGED CHECKS
Berkeley Merchants Victim
ized by Man Who Uses
Name of Charles F. Cole
FLOOD OF BOGUS PAPER
Â».
Clumsy Counterfeit Accepted
. From Schemer, Who Gets
Good Coin in Exchange
a
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
' . 2148 Center Street, Jan. 6.
A flood of forged checks, passed upon
merchants ot Berkeley during the last
few days, came to the attention of the
police to-day when four of the victims
related their tales of woe, and showed
in evidence the bits of worthless pa
per which they took in exchange for
coin. All the forged checks were on
the University Bank and were drawn
â¢ to the orVer of " J. Woerdt."
The name of C. F. Cole, agent for the
Spring Construction Company in this
.part of the country, was signed to
the '-hecks. The company has an ac
count at the University Bank, of which
account Cole is made agent. The per
son perpetrating the forgeries evident
ly was familiar with this fact, but neg
' letted to make his checks out in the
manner adopted by <'ole, who Is wont
to issue a c heck signed by "The Spring
Construction Company, per C. F. Cole."
John Sweetman, proprietor of the
Chicago Hotel at West Berkeley, was
â¢ one of the victims. He accepted a
. check from a stranger, who had bought
\. a few drinks at the bar of his hotel,
â¢ and in other ways had made himself
known as a "good fellow." The strang
. er's expenditures were not large, but
they gave him a certain standing and
'. when he asked that his "pay check" be
cashed, Sweetman obligingly favored
him with $25, a part of which immedi
â¢ ately was spent over the counter. When
' the check came to Cashier Mills at the
: University Bank of Berkeley he
promptly declared that the name at the
â¢, ' bottom did not represent any account
in the University Bank.
Fred Munday, proprietor of the Roy
al Arch saloon at San Pablo and Uni
. versity avenues, was the second vlc
. ' Tim of the check passer to make his
trouble known. He received one of the
documents which "J. Woerdt" so
thoughtfully distributed in West
. Berkeley. The amount inscribed on the
bit of paper was identical with that
which Mr. Sweetman paid, and the
. â¢ check met a similar fate when it came
â¢ - to the bank for payment. James
Grady, bartender, cashed the check at
Munday's place for "Woerdt." He will
â¢.'have to stand the loss.
The police expect to hear from other
. merchants of Berkeley who have been
victimized by the smooth stranger who
â¢ picked up the money so easily in West
Berkeley. They declare that it is usu
ally the plan for swindlers of this sort
â â¢to cut a wider swath than four bogus
checks would represent. Inquiry is
.being made among merchants for
."traces of the swindler.
John Sweetman, one of the victims,
said: "The man who got the money
from me represented himself as a la
borer, who had money coming to him
from the quarry people near here. His
. checks were made out in regular fash
ion. They looked like pay checks. I
had no hesitation in cashing them,
. though I had only known Woerdt, as
.he called himself, a few days."
Fred Munday tells a similar story.
A third victim of the forger appeared
at the West Berkeley Bank this after
noon, in the person of a saloon-keeper,
whose place of business is at Ninth
street and San Pablo avenue. His
â  name was not given out at the bank,
but he is reported to have lost $5
through the check passer's operations.
Charles F. Cole, agent for the Spring
Construction Company, resides at 2417
Bancroft way. He said to-night: "I
..have been Informed that my name has
been forged to at least four checks that
have been accepted by merchants of

I hope the swindler will be caught and

CHURCH CLOSES FIRST
HALF CENTURY.
EXERCISES AT ST. JOHN'S
OAKLAND, Jan. Â«.âThe celebration
of the fiftieth anniversary of the or
ganization of St. John's Episcopal
Church of Oakland began this morning
with a parochial communion of the
members of the parish. The Rev. Ed
gar F. Gee, rector, conducted the ser
vice and afterward addressed the con
gregation. Many of those baptised or
confirmed at St. John's Church during
the half-century of its work, who have
since moved away from the parish, at
tended the service this*morning, once
more meeting old friends.
The first of the regular jubilee ser
vices was held this evening, being a
union meeting of all the Episcopal par
ishes of Alameda County, with all the
rectors of these parishes participating.
The Right Rev. William Ford Nichols,
Bishop of the diocese, preached the
opening jubilee sermon, in which he
told of the great work of Dr. Benja
min Akerly, founder of St John's,
during the forty years of his rector
ship.
After the reading of the lesson by the
Rev. C. T. Walkley, rector of St. Paul's,
the Rev. Edgar F. Gee welcomed the
clergymen and laity to the church.
After announcing the time of the vari
ous Jubilee services the rector said:
To this our golden Jubilee celebration I bid
ycu welcome. Especially do I extend a hearty
welcome to the people who after a long ab
sence from the parish have come once more to
the old home. For this Is home, fthe first home o
our church In Alameda County. St. Haul s
Church, the Church oUÂ« Advent in East Oak
land. St. Andrew s, Trinity and all the other
Kpit.copal churches in Alarieda County are the
children of St. John"s, and in the same sense
Â«re the result of the great work of Dr. Akerly,
the beloved founder and father of our parish.
The address of the rector was fol
lowed by the opening anniversary ad
dress of Bishop Nichols, who took as
his text, "Let your light so shine be
fore men that they may see your good
works, and glorify your Father which
is in heaven." After speaking at some
length of the history of St. John's
Church and parish in the work of the
diocese, Bishop Nichols said:
In the course of these golden jubilee services
you are to have a historical address on the
great work of St. Paul s parish during its fifty
years, and you will hear a part of what may
be told of the life of Dr. Akerly, its founder.
But it seems to me that to-night I may take
that life, which for four-fifths of the life of
this parish was devoted to Its welfare, and
the. welfare of Its people, as an example of
my text.
Benjamin Akerly waÂ» a pioneer, and, as
your rector has said, he waa expansive. Dr.
Akerly believed that one of the best ways to
insure the growth and prosperity of this par
ish was to build up others around it, and to
day we see the result of his work in the other
churches of Alameda County. But his work
was not easy. Dr. Akerly was a pioneer, and
be met the same difficulties'as confront others
engaged in pioneer work, But with the true
pioneer spirit he conquered these difficulties
and layed the foundations on which we have
since built
Holy communion will be celebrated
to-morrow morning at 7:30 o'clock in
memory of departed members of the
parish. On Sunday elaborate services
will be held.

FINDS MISSING WARES
IN UPSTAIRS FLAT
Housewife Plays Detective and Gets
Clew That Neighbor Had Bagged
Many Domestic Articles.
OAKLAND, Jan. 6.âFriends as well
as neighbors were Mrs. P. Walcott and
Mrs. Mary Palmer, occupying flats at
1402 Thirteenth street, until Mrs. Wal
cctt missed a quantity of her house
hold effects and suspected her upstairs
neighbor of knowing something about
the disappearance.
Sheets, pillows, towels, table cloths,
aprons and underskirts do not take
wings of themselves and fly away,
conned Mrs. Walcott. There had been
no burglar a-prowling around at night
to the housewife's knowledge. She
did a bit of Sherlock Holmesing. The
result was an application for a search
warrant upon an affidavit that Mrs.
Palmer had made way with the stuff.
Detectives searched the Palmer flat
and recovered much domestic para
phernalia. In the Police Court to-day
Mrs. Walcott identified half a dozen
sheets, a dozen pillow cases, a dozen
tea doylies, a dosen hand towels, a
table cloth, two dozen napkins, one
gold charm, two aprons and one flan
nel underskirt.
Mrs. Palmer made no resistance, nor
did she appear to question the owner
ship of the goods.
POLICEMAN KEEPS MAIL
CARRIER OUT OF OFFICE
A. R. H. Wolff Would Go to Work, De
spite Order Suspending Him
for Five Days.
ALAMEDA, Jan. 6.âWhen Letter
Carrier Alfred R. H. Wolff, suspended
for five days for refusing to return to
a postal inspector charges that had
been preferred against him, attempted
to enter the employes' door of the
postoffice to-day he was confronted by
Policeman Edward McDonnell and or
dered to stay out or lay himself liable
to arrest. Wolff was accused some
weeks ago of using abusive and dis
respectful language to persons on the
route where he carried mail and
charges were lodged against him. He
was served with a copy of the same by
a postal inspector and when the lat
ter demanded the return of the docu
ment yesterday Wolff refused; to com
ply. Postmaster T. W. Leydecker was
then ordered to suspend Wolff for five
days for Insubordination. It was his
effort to go to work despite the order
of suspension that resulted in Wolffs
meeting with a policeman.

ANOTHER WIDOW
IS VICTIMIZED
Portland Woman Deserted by
"Doc" Proctor on Eve of
Wedding Day Is Accuser
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 6.
Another widow, this time from Ore
gon, has arisen to accuse "Doctor"
William Proctor, now in the County
Jail, accused of Inveigling aged and
confiding widows to loan him money
upon the prospect of his accompanying
them to Hymen's altar. Her name is
Sarah Clark and she writes from Port
land, Ore. The story is the same as
told by the others, although the
amount obtained by Proctor is not
stated. She says that nothing was too
small for him to steal and that he
went off with a bolt of silk out of
which a wedding gown was to have
been made.
Mrs. Clark writes that she is glad to
know where Proctor is so that she can
write to him and tell him what she
thinks of him, with a warning that if
they ever meet she will make the
meeting memorable. She refers in a
jocular way to the marriage license
she bought and tells how Proctor left
town on a night train on the eve of
their wedding day. She mourns still
the loss of the contents of her purse
and the silk, which she had kept as a
treasure.
Proctor and Roy Ashberry, his step
son and alleged confederate, were,
taken to Centerville to-day for the
continuation of the preliminary hear
ing on the charge of stealing diamond
rings from aged Mrs. Tyson of Nlles.
Owing to the absence of thoir attorney,
Tom Bradley, however, the case was
postponed until next Tuesday.
YOUNG WEBER'S COUNSEL
ATTACKS INDICTMENT
Demurs to True Bill Charging Rob
bery Returned by the Grand Â»
Jury.
AUBURN, Jan. 6.âThe case of
Adolph Weber, charged with robbing
the Placer County Bank, came up on
demurrer before Judge Prewett tc-day.
Attorney Tabor, in his demurrer, at
tacked the indictment on the ground
that it did not charge that the money
of which the bank waa robbed was tak
erf from the person of Bookkeeper Ed
ward McPadyen or froni his immediate
presence, but that it simply stated the
money was taken from his possession.
He argued that to constitute robbery
the money must have been taken from
the person, otherwise it would be grand
larceny.
The demurrer was taken under ad
visement by the court.
WANT BILLOTS
TO BE SECRET
Socialists Would Enjoin the
Calling of Primary Elec
tion Under the Present Law
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 6.
In order to prepare the way for per
sons to vote the Socialist ticket at the
primary election to be held the latter
part of this month without being
obliged to indicate their party affilia
tion, arguments were made before
Judge Henry Melvin this afternoon for
an injunction restraining, the City
Council from calling an election under
the present primary law upon the
ground that it is unconstitutional.
The action is begun by M. Lesser,
who was a Socialist candidate for Con
gress at the last election, and he was
represented by Attorneys Lewis and
Royce, who hold that the present law
is unconstitutional upon three grounds
âfirst, because it is not secret; second,
because it discriminates between cit
izens in that one having been a dele
gate to one convention cannot sign a
petition for another; third, because it
Interferes with the rights of citizens
to associate together as. political par
ties and instruct delegates. City At
torney McElroy argued for the city.
The arguments took a wide range and
bear upon the point that by rea
son of the fact that the ballot is not
secret many persons are deterred from
voting the Socialist ticket on the
ground that small political parties are
looked on with ill favor.
Judge Melvin took the case under
advisement.

DIVORCE RONS
IN THE FAMILY
Auzerais' Name Again Ap
pears on Record of Courts
in Suit to Sever the Ties
RECALLS HOT CONTEST
Man of Pioneer Connections
Made Defendant in a Com
plaint Alleging Desertion
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 6.
Eva E. Auzerais has sued Raoul Auze
rais In San Francisco for a divorce,
alleging desertion. The couple have
been separated for two years. Auzerais
is a member of the pioneer San Jose
family of that name, and a brother to
Louis Auxerais, whose domestic affairs
were involved in sensational litigation
in the Alameda County courts. Louis
Auzerais had been divorced from his
wife, who was the daughter of Llew
ellyn Williams, a prominent capitalist
of Sacramento. She had been married
several times. By Auzerais there was
a son, John Llewellyn Auzerais, who
was the subject of a battle for guard
ianship.
The boy by his mother's will was
left a fortune of $150,000. Esmoil, the
stepfather; Mrs. Auzerais of San Jose,
his grandmother, and C. W. Clark,
trustee of the boy's estate, made a
three-cornered fight to be named guard
ian. Esmoil won. The boy's father had
remarried and was in Paris. He took
no part in the contest.
Raoul Auzerais left San Francisco
two years ago and is supposed to be
in Chicago. His wife resides in San
Francisco.
Thomas J. Mitchell has begun a suit
for divorce from Anna M. Mitchell,
whom he accuses of cruelty and de
sertion. Besides being a scold he says
that once when she got the house
painted she told him it cost $200 when
in reality it only cost $125 and she pock
eted the difference.
The following interlocutory decrees
were granted to-day: Frances
Braendli from Henry Braendli for de
sertion; Mary A. Russell from John
Russell for extreme cruelty; Emma
Matheson from Joseph Matheson for
extreme cruelty; Anna P. Miguel from
John P. Miguel for extreme cruelty;
Hulda Smyth from Robert J. Smyth
for desertion.
NOT TO REPEAT
QUEER BLUNDER
Survey Ordered of University
Campus to Make Impos
sible Repetition of Error
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 6.
Under the direction of John Galen
Howard, professor of architecture at
the State University, surveyors to-day
began the work of making an accurate
and comprehensive survey of the entire
university campus, the result of which
is expected to be a prevention of any
errors in the making of plans for struc
tures thereon, such, for example, as
was made when the plans for the min
ing building were carried out.
At that time if Architect Benard's
ideas had been carried out the mining
building would have been left with one
corner about forty feet up in the air.
This was due to the failure to supply
the famous French architect with just
such a map or survey as is now being
made. A glance at the mining building
as it now stands, uncompleted, is said
by architects to reveal the original mis
conception of the ground elevation by
the architect who made the plans for
the building.
John Galen Howard Is to be supplied
with a map so fine and complete that
the height of every tree on the campus
will be revealed. The map will show
the elevation of every hillock, and it
will also have a table of comparative
distances, by the aid of which plans for
any of the structures to be built event
ually for the "greater university" can
be drawn hereafter without the possi
bility of an error at any stage of the
proceedings.
Architect Howard has almost finished
the drawings for the new university
library, to cost $600,000, and very soon
work on that magnificent structure is
to be begun. When completed it will
be the best equipped library in the
country. Wisconsin University now has
the largest library building, with most
modern equipment, but the palm is
eventually to be California's when all
the plans now in abeyance are carried
out. It is expected to be sufficient for
the university's needs, according to the
authorities, for seventy-five years to
come.
COYOTES AND WILDCATS
BOTHER BUTTE FARMERS
Animals Make Raids on Chicken and
Turkey Roosts and Appear In
Streets of Town.
CHICO, Jan. 6.âResidents of Chero
kee, as well as the farmers in that vi
cinity, are experiencing great inconven
ience from the raids of coyotes and
wildcats. The animals attack chicken
and turkey roosts. At times in broad
daylight they appear in large numbers
in the very town. So troublesome have
they become that the residents are con
sidering the advisability of petitioning
the Supervisors to place a bounty on
scalps.

ACCEPTS BAY
CITIES' PLAN
Council Makes Move Toward
Calling a Municipal Wa
ter Supply Bond Election
â¢
TO BE HELD IN MARCH
Contra Costa's Condemna
tion Offer Referred Back
Without Recommendation
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway. Jan. 6.
The Bay Cities Water Company's
proposal to Install a municipal water
supply of 20,000,000 gallons daily "ca
pacity for $0,750,000 was accepted to
night by the City Council in commit
tee of the whole. City Attorney Mc-
Elroy was instructed to draw the
necessary resolution for presenta
tion to the City Council Monday night.
The Contra Costa Water Company's
offer to submit to a condemnation suit
to determine the value of its plant was
referred to the City Council without
recommendation.
The Bay Cities' project as definitely
submitted to-night recites the com
pany's readiness to enter Into the
necessary contract with the city as
soon as the bond election shall be held
to authorize the city to proceed.
In its tender the corporation declares
it will furnish a sufficient bond of
$1,000,000 to protect the interests of the
city under the contract. It purposes
to provide the proposed system of wa
ter works, including all necessary
rights, lands, properties and ease
ments, to make all Improvements and
to convey the completed plant to the
city free of all incumbrance.
The company agrees specifically to
meet the following conditions:
To maintain the city's rights to take
and use the storm waters of Isabel
Creek and the Arroyo del Valle; to
maintain the city's right to the peace
able possession and use of all the prop
erties, rights and easements included In
the water works system; to complete
the specified, or other agreed improve
ments upon the properties, in a sub
stantial and workmanlike manner; to
deliver the water works system to the
city free and clear of all incumbrance.
The company requires that a reso
lution accepting the offer shall be
adopted within ten days, and that with
in twenty-flve days thereafter a bond
election shall be called. If the bonds
carry, the company stipulates that the
contract must be signed by March 31.
Accompanying the proposal was an in
vitation to each member of the Coun
cil to make a thorough investigation of
all the data in the company's posses
sion.
Dates were submitted showing that
the election can be called between
March 1 and 13.
Attorney R. M. Fitzgerald, in reply to
Councilman Howard's questions, said
he would on Monday night furnish the
City Council with a statement concern
ing the company's position in the mat
ter of title to the watershed. HowaN
said he thought the company should be
willing to pay the cost of the bond elec
tion in the event the titles are not ac
ceptable to the city.
The Spring Valley Water Works gave
notice it would attack the right of the
Bay Cities to Isabel Creek waters. At
torney Fitzgerald replied that the com
pany fully expected and was pre
pared to meet that litigation. He de
clared the bond of $1,000,000 was by no
means the extent of the corporation's
resources, and that it was fully sup
plied with the means to give the city
what it had offered to supply.

FIST FIGHT IN ROOMS
OF MAYOR OF PORTLAND
Ex-City Official and Indicted Contrac
tor Come to Blows After Ex.
cntlve Board's Meeting.
PORTLAND, Ore., Jan. 6.â"You're a
robber," cried E. W. Riner in Mayor
Williams' office this afternoon. He
spoke to J. M. Long, ex-City Attorney,
who a moment before had represented
the Oregon Savings Bank before the
meeting of the executive board in the
room adjoining. And then and there
occurred a fierce fist fight, for Riner
struck Long a tremendous blow, which
knocked the lawyer nearly across the
room.
Fire Chief Campbell and members of
the august executive board rushed into
the Mayor's office and separated the
combatants, who lunged repeatedly at
each other while in the peacemakers'
arms.
Riner and his father were the con
tractors on the notorious Tanner Creek
sewer. The bank represented by Long
financed the contract. The Riners haV e
since been indicted by the county
Grand Jury.
Gold to Go to Japan
NEW YORK, Jan. 6.âA million dol
lars in gold certificates were depos
ited in the United States Sub-Treasury
in this city to-day by a New .York firm
of brokers, who will draw a similar
amount in gold bars from the San
Francisco Mint for shipment to Japan.

LINER ACAPULCO IS ATTACKED BY A WHALE
w
Big Fish Charges on Steamship, but an Upper
Cut From Propeller Makes Leviathan Quit.
PACIFIC MAIL COMPANY*? LINER CHINA, WHICH WILL SAIL. TUESDAY FOR THE ORIENT, CARRYING A LARGE
CARGO FOR FAR EASTERN POBTS AND TAKING FOR THE JAPANESE GOVERNMENT A SHIPMENT OF GOLD
BULLION VALUED AT $2,500,000.
The Pacific Mall Company's Ilii*r
Acapulco, which arrived early yester
day morning- from Panama and way
ports, had an exciting experience with
a whale off San Bias. The leviathan,
floating high out of the water, was en
joying a morning nap and giving its
tough hide a sun bath when the Aca
pulco came along. The lookout sighted
the whale and called the attention of
the man at the wheel to the slumbering
mountain of flesh that floated directly
in the liner's path. The quartermaster,
who was steering, gave the spokes of
the wheel a twist and the Acapulco's
head sheered off a little. A collision
was avoided, but the Acapulco's side
grazed the starboard shoulder of the
big fish and jarred the whale from
slumberland.
The whale was fully awake before the
liner had entirely passed. He awoke in
a bad temper and made a furious rush
at the Acapulco's stern. The whale
found the disturber of his dreams a
pretty solid sort of flsh, but, undaunt
ed by his failure to ram his head
through the steel plates, gathered him
self for another charge. He struck the
steamer under the stern, and as he
bounced off slashed at the retreating
hull with his tail. Then one more rush.
This time the whale found his match,
for the rapidly revolving propeller
landed a bewildering succession of up
percuts on his lower jaw. With a splash
of defiance the leviathan dived and dis
appeared and the people of the Aca
pulco saw him no more. Chief Officer
Bailey interviewed the quartermaster,
who had avoided the whale by such a
narrow margin, and concluded a heart
to-heart sailor talk by advising the
steersman in future to "let sleeping
whales He."
The new lighthouse at Cape San Lu
cas was in commission, although not
quite finished, when the Acapulco
passed. The light was burning and
Captain Brown reports very favorably
on the new beacon, .which for many
years has been wanted by mariners.
The Acapulco brought 980 tons of car
go and treasure valued at $2300. She
brought forty-one passengers, among
whom were the following:
John Metly, E. H. Moffett. J. Davis, H.
Beckett. Lam Chin, O. L. Dyson, I, W. Han
â¢on. Marguerite Davie, Madeline Holland, M.
I. Wonderlick. H. N. Butler. George Park
hurst. G. Davis. Roy Holland. Beatrice Won
derlick.
â¢
Will Carry Much Gold.
In addition to a large cargo the Pacific
Mail Company's liner China, which sails on
Tuesday for the Orient, will carry to Japan
$-.500,000 In gold bullion. Although no longer
enjoying the distinction of being the biggest,
the China Is still the handsomest of Pacific
Mail liners, and many travelers prefer her to
her younger, bigger sisters, The China has
a fine record for regularity and probably the
roomiest passenger accommodations of any
vessel out of this port.
The taking off of the liner Gaelic and the
consequent readjustment of the Joint schedule
will Invoke some hard work for the Pacific
Mail Company's dock force. The Doric, the
next vessel due. should be here January 13.
The Manchuria, however, due after the Doric,
will call before the O. and O. boat. The Man
churia is scheduled to call January 21. but
it Is not likely that she will. In order to get
her home as speedily as possible she will
omit calling at Shanghai and Honolulu. Bhe
will come direct frem Yokohama. She has
not yet reached the Japanese port, however,
and as the run will occupy at least twelve
days, the margin for discharging and reload
ing will be too narrow to admit of her being
ready for eea again by January 21. The Doric
will not sail until February 2.
Hawaii Is Still on Fire.
A cable received yesterday from Kobe con
tains the Information that the barkentlne Ha
waii is still on Are, but that the flames are
under control. The Hawaii reached Kobe the
other day with a cargo of fertilizer. She had
a rough trip, and her cargo had been on fire
during the voyage. Eleven feet of mater,
pumped Into her hold, was supposed to have
extinguished tbe fire, and so it was reported
on the vessel's arrival. This now appears to
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL", SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1905.
have been a mistake, but as the vessel it in
port there is thought to be lWtle danger of
her sustaining any serious damage. ,
British Ship Uninsurable.
The finding of two life-preservers from the
overdue British ship Edith Mary, now out
105 days from Ascension for St. Johns, re
sulted yesterday in the vessel being declared
uninsurable. She was quoted at 85 per cent.
The only vessel left In the overdue list is
the British bark Stork, whose chances are
considered slight. She Is out 109 days from
Hudson Bay for London and Is quoted at 70
per cent.
Athos Reaches Colon.
The steamship Athos, among whose passen
gers are fifty delegates from Baltimore to the
Pan-American Medical Congress at Panama,
arrived yesterday at Colon. The vessel was
four days overdue, and her failure to arrive
caused considerable anxiety.
Sea Rover Has Lakme in Tow.
The steam schooner North Fork arrived yes
terday from Eureka, from which port she
started with the waterlogged steamir Ijkme
in tow. The tug Sea Rover relieved the North
Fork of her tow at 10 o'clock Thursday morn
ing about fifteen miles northwest of Point
Arena. The Rover should have the Lakme
In port this morning.

NOTEâ-In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time; tbe
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day. except when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are in
addition to the soundings of the United States
Coast Survey Charts, except when a minus (â)
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given is subtracted from the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference is the mean
of the lower low waters.

CITY VICTOR
OVER STATE
Judge Seawell Determines
Right to Collect Taxes
From United Railroads
MONEY IS ON DEPOSIT
Before Payment Is Finally
Made Question Will Go
to the Supreme Court
The conflicting" claims between the
State andrthis city and county as to
the right of collecting taxes from the
United Railroads were decided in favor
of the local government yesterday by
Judge Seawell, and will be at once car
ried to the Supreme Court for final de
termination.
The purchase of the San Francisco
and San Mateo electric line by the
United Railroads was the sticking point
in the controversy. State Controller
E. P. Colgan contended that in making
the inter-county route a part of the
United Railroads system the company
was brought under the law relating to
the taxation of railroads operating in
two or more counties. Tax Collector E.
J. Smith of this county held that the
United Railroads was a local corpora
tion, and its property, including fran
chise, equipment, etc., should be locally
taxed up to the county line.
Though pprfectly willing to pay its
taxes to whomsoever they were due,
the United Railroads did not want to
become liable for double'payment by
making thf mistake of paying to the
wrong claimant, and therefore the mat
ter was brought to court with the de
mand that the conflicting parties
should set forth their respective claims.
Colgan and Smith responded, as did
Collector Frank M. Granger of San Ma
teo County and M. F. Boland of San
Mateo city. The railroads, pending the
decision, gave Judge Seawell a check
for $364,572 79. This was the county's
assessment, as against 1331,579 30 de
manded by the State Controller.
By Judge Seawell's decision the city
gains about $33,000.
SANTA FE SENDS GLAD
TIDINGS TO LAUNDRYMEN
Issues Edict Against Use of Celluloid
and Rubber Collars by Its
Trainmen.
Rubber and celluloid collars have
been tabooed by the Santa Fe Com
pany. According to a circular issued
from the office of Trainmaster E. A.
Austin at Topeka, It will hereafter be
a serious offense for a trainman to
edorn his neck with one of these pro
ducts of a gigantic trust.
Heretofore the conductors and
brakemen of Santa Fe trains have
found it convenient to go to a wash
room and clean their waterproof col
lars after a long and dusty run. It
meant the saving of much laundry ex
pense. Naturally their faces are
wearing mournful expressions since
the edict. Hereafter they will be
compelled to carry in their valises ex
tra collars. Trainmaster Austin's or
der, copies of which have just been
received at this end of the line, reads
as follows:
My attention has recently been called to the j
fact that some of our passenger trainmen are
Wearing rubber and celluloid collars. This Is
a practice that the management will not per
mit, and you will be governed accordingly.
The Santa Fe, as well as other j
transcontinental railroads, has fixed |
rules relative to the uniforms of its
trainmen, and it deems it within its
province to dictate what kind of hab- \
erdashery its employes shall wear.

LOCAL CEMENT
GOOD ENOUGH
Domestic Product Is Adyo
cated in Place of Im
ported Material by Dealers
STATE LAW PROPOSED
Dealers Desire That Pub
lic Buildings Be Made
to Foster an Industry
The cement men of the State are
about to introduce a bill in the Legis
lature for the purpose of fostering the
cement industry of California. Import
ed cements are being used on a number
of buildings being put up at public ex
pense, and the effort will be to have
the bill framed so as to prevent the
use of anything but State or United
States material in public structures.
| The cement men, of whom Colonel
j George Stone, chairman of the Repub
| lican State Central Committee is a
; prominent member, think that Califor
j nia cement should be used in the con-
I struction of California Hall at the Uni
versity of California. Counter claims
are made that the imported cement is
better adapted for certain class of
work. However, the local cement deal
ers believe that it will be a saving of
about 75 cents a barrel if the Solano or
San Joaquin cement can be substituted
for ihat imported from England.
There has not been much competi
! tion, however, with the foreign cement
| market of late. Shipments to Califor
i nia have not been largeânot more than
one-fourth of the volume of four years
ago. There has been a very good class
of cement mined In this State and the
promoters of the "cement bill" want to
j foster the Industry, so they declare.

HAS PROSPEROUS TEAR
! Preparing for Addition to Institution
Officers Appeal for Donations of
Articles of Furniture.
The officers of the Beulah Rescue
Home, conducted under the auspices
of the Salvation Army, report that
I during the year of 1904 106 girls
! passed through the home, 92 per cent
j proving^satisfactory cases. The home
is now overfull and another house de
voted to rescue work has been kindly
given. This will mean much extra ex
pense for the furnishing and the of
ficers make an appeal for the contribu
tion of the articles most needed and
which are: Twelve iron bedsteads
(single, high ones preferred); mat
tresses, pillows, blankets, comforters
and bed linen; small tables, wash
stands or dressers; chairs, bowls.
pitchers, and towels; dining-room
table, chairs, dishes and table linen;
kitchen table and utensils; two entire
bedroom suits, some small rugs and
suitable furniture for office and recep
tion rooms and a good sewing machine
and typewriter.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL
lOHX D. BPRJHXTKKLS ...,..Â»<. Proprietor
ADDRESS ALL, COMMUNICATIONS TO
.JOHN McNAUGHT Manager
t BLICATTON OFFICE THIRD AND MARKET STREETS, SAN FRANCISCO j
SATURDAY JANUARY 7, 1905 j
'HpHE joint Congressional commission has made a unanimous re-
I port, accompanied by a bill for an act covering the subject of
* the restoration of the American merchant marine. The com
mission visited all the leading seaports on both coasts and took the
testimony of ship owners, ship builders, shippers, marine under
. writers and all interested parties. The investigation was very com
' prehensive, and it is gratifying that the members of both parties
making it have been able to reach an agreement.
Heretofore partisan differences have much hindered a proper
consideration of this important subject. All business men agreed
that the diversion of $100,000,000 a year to pay foreign sfcips for
doing our deep water trade is a waste of the national resources,
and that the lack of an adequate merchant marine was eliminating
the seagoing character of our people. This is a serious matter for
a country with the longest coast line in the world, and with a for
eign trade by sea of such immense volume as ours. But what all
business men agreed upon was the subject of partisan differences,
which it may be hoped will now disappear in the presence of this
unanimous report.
The bill provides a graduated tonnage bounty, running from
$2 50 to $5 per ton, according to the time the ship is employed in
commerce, and for mail contracts on both oceans. A tonnage tax of
8 and 16 cents per ton on foreign ships entering our ports is pro
vided for, this tax not to exceed 80 cents and $1 60 per ton in any
one year on the same ship. The tonnage tax now runs from 3to 6
cents per ton. The increase is intended to give the preference to
American ships. It may not prove of importance, as the maritime
nations with which we tr*ade may impose a tax of equal amount on
American ships, thus equalizing the influence of this tax upon freight
rates. The proposed bill differentiates this tonnage tax by levying
8 cents on ships entering our ports from any foreign port in North
America, the West Indies, Bahamas, the Caribbean coast of South
America or Newfoundland, and 16 cents per ton on ships entering
â¢our ports from any other foreign country. On ships of the latter
class the annual total tax shall not exceed $1 60 per ton.
One of the important provisions of the bill is for American ap
prentices on ships that receive its benefits. These apprentices are to
be trained in navigation and engineering. The apprentices are*to be
one for the ship and one for each thousand of her net registered
tonnage. This will give on ships of the class of our "Pacific liners
from ten to fifteen American apprentice boys, who are trained as
able seamen, navigators and engineers, and are qualified for service
in the navy and to become masters of ships. This is an extremely de
sirable feature of the law. There has been felt a doubt whether
American boys will seek a seafaring life. But their aversion may
run to existing conditions under which there is no regular system
of apprenticeship and boys on shipboard are left to learn seaman
ship at haphazard. That American boys will seek a seagoing life
seems to be proved by the large number that enlist as cadets in the
.various naval training schools and ships. There they are under
conditions that give them discipline and knowledge, and though in
the navy they can go no higher than the position of yeoman the
applications for admission overtax the facilities of the schools. When
they can article on a merchant ship under conditions quite as favor
able for discipline and training and. can hope to qualify themselves
â¢as officers and shipmasters, we are persuaded that they will im
prove their opportunity.
In the day of the old wooden ships there was no lack of native
American sailors. The sea life was then a lure anÂ«i the place to
hunt for a runaway boy was on board ship. This spirit declined
with our merchant marine, and the so-called American sailors on
our ships are 90 per cent foreigners. It may be truthfully said
that a revival of our merchant marine will fail of one of its important
purposes if it be not accompanied by a revival of the seagoing spirit
of our people. Therefore this provision for apprentices is wise and
in line with the whole movement to put our flag again over the seas
and to send our commerce in American bottoms.
THE SUNDAY CALL FICTION CONTEST.
PROBABLY no feature ever instituted by a daily paper has
leaped so quickly into popular favor or aroused interest in so
unexpected and so wide a field as the fiction contest for West
ern writers which was inaugurated in the Sunday Call two months
ago. It is no extravagance to state that practically every county in
the State of California has been represented by contributions many
times over, and, more surprising still, practically every State in
the Union. During the current week manuscripts were received
from eleven States outside of California, and the coast States, the
manuscripts coming respectively from Madison, S. D.; Chilicothe,
Wyo.; Merriwell, Wis.; Wichita, Kans.; Falls City, Nebr.; Haver
hill, Mass.; Mankato. Mich.; Beloit, Wis.; Lake Mills, Wis.; Jack
son, Term.; Easton, Pa.; Waterford, Buffalo and other points in New
York State, notably one from a West Point army officer.
Of course the contest is what the advertisement defines, a fiction
contest for Western writers, and most of the contributors from
abroad justify their entry of manuscripts upon the ground that they
are residents of California who have drifted East. But it is inter
esting and significant to note the widespread interest taken in a
.contest for original fiction instituted by a daily paper in view of
the hidebound belief that has so long been maintained that only the
â¢ standard magazines and weeklies can conduct contests of this nature
successfully. It only serves to emphasize what the Sunday Call has
long maintained: that the old sensational supplement is a thing of
the past, and in its stead has come a cleaner, saner, more whole
some and more literary weekly magazine.
In point of number the contributors have been legion. Not only
new writers, paving the way to break into the magazine field, but
writers already famous have'contributed material for selection. The
prize story for the past week is from the pen of Hallie Erminie
Rives, author of "The Castaway," a writer whose work has probably
never before been submitted for original publication in a daily paper.
But the majority of selections will be made from the contributions
of the newer writers; an occasional story from an experienced writer
will only serve to show how high the standard of the contest is. The
work of new writers, however strong and original, often needs some
slight charity toward overlooking the crudities that inevitably go
with inexperience. It is in this liberal spirit that the present contest
is being conducted. < ________________

INDIAN SUMMER
THE barbaric red of the parasol
which she presently unfurled
when with a sort of aimless re-
luctance she had passed down
the steps and out Into the Septem
ber sunshine formed a vivid back
ground for the small, well-set head
and face which was at once humor
ous and quizzical, as If the pageant
of life had raised in her an eternal
question and an accompanying smile.
At the distance of half a block the
trim figure in its severe white pique
seemed .ail girl. A closer approach,
however, showed in the wavy dark
hair isolated silver threads âthose out
riders of Time which come to lay low
the loveliest.
And, indeed, only a few days before
two placid matrons who had cast off
their girlish slimness as a snake does
its skin and achieved in its place solid
ity and double chins might have been
heard discussing her case.
"Strange that Elizabeth Ware doesn't
marry!" observed one, as with eyes,
bent upon her embroidery hoop she
caused a strange floral piece to bloom
upon a piece of white linen. "She's
getting on. Let me see. She was in
the next class to me at Madame Dore's,
and Harold will be 14 next October!"
Harold was her eldest childâone of
those cantankerous pegs by which ma
trons keep tab on the age of their
unmarried friends.
"She must be nearly 35 if she's a
day," the speaker concluded after a
brief pause, in which she debated with
herself what would be the proper
shade of green to use for maidenhair
ft m. "Her time is growing short."
Her companion, engaged in a simi
lar employment, looked meditatively
off over the smooth-shaven lawn
which seemed to share her own do
mestic placidity.
"Thirty-four, I think," she an
nounced in a voice whose preoccupa
tion suggested that she had been rum
maging in that prehistoric past which
contained neither husband nor babies.
"ThUrty-four, I'm pretty sure. Still,
she certainly doesn't look It, and she's
always had plenty of attention."
In the absorbing interest of the
flowers they were fashioning the sub
ject was dropped, but the thought
wave then started reached Elizabeth
Ware herself later on.
It was a wonderful day, full of mel
low, mature, Indescribable charm.
"Summer grows wise," Elizabeth
thought to herself; "no longer daz
zling and shriveling, but flooding the
world with tender light, veiling its
splendors in mists."
The strong, white, ungloved hands
twirled the parasol thoughtfully while
their owner drew in a deep breath.
There was something in the day that
stirred memories, brought back half
forgotten flirtations, filled her thoughts
with a vague, beautiful regretâthe
loss of something she had never
known.
Somewhere about a locust shrilled
its warning; of the frosts to come.
Miss Ware's eyes grew dreamy, pen
sive with that questioning of the fu
ture which will disturb the mind of
the unmated woman until the last
fence Is reached.
Had she, perchance, made a mis
take? She thought of her school
friends wrapped in contentment âand
flesh âwho no longer seemed contem
poraries. They had secured their
matrimonial prizes. Yes, and sat down
by the roadside of life, with nb desire
to fare farther! So far as she knew
all were mildly happy. But beside
them she felt ridiculously young and
joyous. A smile touched her lips as
she reflected that it was with Harold,
the 14-year-old that she preferred to
walk and talk, rather than with Har
old's plump and settled mamma.
Then she reviewed the thirty-five
years that stretched behind her, flam
ing like the tail of a comet, with
variety, with pleasant wanderings,
with an ever-changing group of men

new class for Chinese
School Director Roncovieri
to Be Continued as Presi
dent by His Associates

The Board of Education is preparing
. to piace at least ten new teachers on
. xhe day substitute list, taking them
from the eligible list of successful con
testants in the last competitive exam
ination. There have been already fifty
two teachers assigned from the latter
list and It is expected that before the
next competitive examination for
â¢ . ieachers is held next June seventy-flve
teachers will have been appointed.
Tho board is instituting inquiries
among teachers as to their desires In
the matter of leaves of absence, In or
der that a sufficient number of substi
â¢ tutes may be selected for the opening
â¢ of the spring term on January 16, the
winter term, under the new order, be
ing concluded on January 13. The
names of the ten standing the next
highest on the eligible list have not
been made public and probably will
not be until the board meets next
Thursday or at a special meeting to be
called at an earlier date. The assign
ments and transfers of a score of
teachers will also be made at the same
. time.
The board will also establish one or
two more classes where the attendance
is very congested. It is contemplated
to form a new class in the Chinese
Primary School, where the principal
reports an increasing number of pu
Â° pils. .
As School Director Altmann will con
tinue in the place by grace of Mayor
Schmits there will be no special cere
* monies in connection with his rein
ductlon to office. The board will meet
informally and proceed to the election
of a president for the ensuing year.
â¢It is understood that Roncovieri will
be continued as such by his associates.
Director Boyle, having but one more
year to serve would be entitled to the
presidency, but elects to withdraw In
favor of Roncovieri, whom he believes
to be deserving of the honor in view
of his zealous efforts in behalf of the
welfare of the public schools.

POSTUM CEREAL.
GOT ALONG.
A Pretty Girl of 17 With Energy.
"During the winter of 1901-02 (I was
then 17 years of age) I attended a
grammar school in Rolla, N. Dak.,
staying with some friends of ours and
â¢working for my board between school
hours.
"I used to get up early in the morn
ing to do my housework, but as those
people got up very late I would not
,cook breakfast but only make the cof
fee. Tired of working and in a hurry
to go to school I would eat but a snack
and a cup of strong coffee for my
breakfast and it was not long until
I got to be nervous, had headaches
and could not sleep.
"One day our Professor Young was
telling us what effect coffee had on
one's aystem. I compared his words
with how 1 felt and found that coffee
was ruining my health. So I tried to
quit but found I was a slave to it,
but quit Just the same. I did not
know what to drinkâl hated milk or
tea, so I would drink nothing but
water; got poor and thin and finally
left school to remove to Belma, Wash.,
where at present, by the constant use
of Postum, you can find me a bright,
healthy girl. After we settled down
papa opened a grocery store.
"At this time I was so ill I could
not do a thing, and mama was anxious
about me. She would prepare some
coffee and try to have me drink it in
place of water, but every time I drank
it I noticed it made me worse. One
day while I was looking around in the
store I noticed your Postum as a food
drink. I asked papa if he knew what
it was, but he said he did not but found
he had to keep it, so I took a package
of it home and said I would try it,
made it according to directions and It
was delicious. After using Postum two
weeks I was much less nervous and
noticed I was getting a little fatter,
so I persuaded mama to use it for the
whole family. Every one of them liked
it and now they all complain if they
cannot have Postum.
"After a while I got to be healthy
and tired of being indoors I went out
to take orders for the store and many
a customer I persuaded to use Postum.
"One lady told me that they used
coffee because they could get premiums
for the wrappers, but she admitted
they did not like coffee at all. I left
them a package of Postum on trial
and the next time I came areund she
returned itâsaid they did not like it.
She hadn't made it right. It was
about dinner time, so I said, 'Let me
make It for you; I am sure you will
like It,' so I nrepared the drink accord
ing to directions and had dinner with
them and they all said it was delicious
and have used nothing else since. That
lady thanked me afterward for Postr
urn; it improved their health, she said."
Name given by Postum Co., Battle
Creek, Mich.
COLLEGE PLAY
WILL BE GIVEN
AT ALHAMBRA
AUGUST AGUIRRE, WHO WON
SUCCESS AS AFFIUS IN "THE
ETERNAL LIGHT."
The students of the Santa Clara Col
lege are making great preparations for
the production of their latest play,
which will be given by them at the
Alhambra Theater on February 8. The
play, which is called "The Light Eter
nal," is the work of Martin V. Merle,
a member of the junior class of the
college. It deals with the rivalry of
two students, afterward soldiers, in
the reign of Emperor Diocletian of
Rome.
A strong character in the production
is Affms, which will be portrayed by
August Aguirre. In the original pro
duction of the play at the college his
success was so great that the young
student won exceptional honors. Many
of the Santa Clara alumni who failed
to see the play at the college will take
advantage of the opportunity to visit
the city and see it at the Alhambra,
More than 150 students will also be
present from the college.
MEMBERS OF THE ALUMNAE
ASSOCIATION PLAN BAZAAR
Graduates of the Sacred Heart Pre-
sentation Convent Arranging
for a May Festival.
The active members of the Alumnae
Association of the Sacred Heart Pre
sentation Convent, Taylor and Ellis
streets, held a meeting in the convent
hall last Wednesday evening for the
purpose of planning a bazaar for the
benefit of the institution. It was re
solved that as a similar fete has not
been held since 1879 the alumnae of
the convent Invite its friends to aid
in this work and make the coming
May festival a social and financial
success.
Persons interested in the work are
requested to call at the convent or
send donations. The next meeting
will take place January 18.
The following young ladles form the
committee of arrangements:
Miss Mary Traynor, Mrs. Urlosta, Miss Mary

patrick, Miss Katie Geggus, Miss Clara No

Commandery of the same order, wish

ternal visit last night in Mission Ma
sonic Hall. Charles L. Field, acting

accepted by the officers of the new
commandery. Addresses were made

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1903.
LEGAL GIANTS
OPEN BATTLE
Contest to Have Dunsmuir
Will Proceedings Thrown
From Court Is Commenced
BIG FIGHT IN PROSPECT
Attorney Campbell Occupies
j Both Sessions of the Court
j in Mrs. Hopper's Interests
The scene of the controversy over the
late Alexander Dunsmuir's millions was
shifted yesterday to Judge Coffey's de
partment of the Superior Court, where
the attorneys for Edna Wallace Hopper
and Joan Olive Dunsmuir, the latter
the mother of the deceased, are seeking
to set aside the judgment of the Supe
rior Court of May 9, 1900, by which
Dunsmuir's will was admitted to pro
bate and his brother James appointed
as executor. In the array of local and
British Columbian legal talent were J.
C. Campbell, representing Mrs. Hop
per; Sir Charles H. Tupper, E. V. Bod
well and Alexander Heyneman, for Mrs.
Dunsmuir; A. P. Luxton and Andrew
Thome, for James Dunsmuir as execu
tor, and Charles S. Wheeler, especially
engaged to represent James Dunsmuir
personally.
If the present proceedings succeed
the way will be opened for an attack
upon the "common form" probate of
the will in British Columbia, and then
will follow the contest of the step
daughter and aged mother of the mil
lionaire against his disposition of the
fortune.
Mr. Campbell opened the proceedings
yesterday by presenting his petition to
set aside the judgment on the ground
that the court had no jurisdiction of
the subject matter of the estate or of
the persons interested. He offered in
evidence an exemplified copy of the will
and the petition of James Dunsmuir
for its admission to probate. Mrs. Hop
per's attorney has three points of at
tack. First, that there is no limit upon
the time in which the judgment may be
revoked, because the judgment was
void in the beginning; second, that the
admission of the will to probate In
common form in British Columbia was
not a sufficient record upon which to
base its admission here without the
usual evidence, and the third, relating
to the jurisdiction of the court in
granting ancillary probate under the
laws of California. The latter question
will be discussed by Mr. Campbell next
Tuesday morning, to which time the
hearing was adjourned.
On the first point, anticipating the
other side would plead that the pro
ceedings were barred, Mr. Campbell
contended that as the judgment was
void in the beginning there couid be no
limitation on the time within which it
could be revoked. He cited authorities
and gave instances wherein several
years had elapsed before proceedings
of this kind were instituted. He then
temporarily surrendered the floor to
Mr. Bodwell, who went into a detailed
explanation to the court of the rules of
practice in British Columbia, particu
larly with relation to the common and
solemn forms of probate. He said that
under the former, as in Dunsmuir's
case, the will was admitted upon the
affidavit of the executor and the order
could be revoked at any time, while
the solemn form could only be revoked
in case of fraud. He quoted English
law and authorities to show that there
was no time limit for the revocation of
probate proceedings and also to prove
that wills must be probated according
to the laws of the country wherein they
were executed.
Mr. Campbell, resuming, then dis
cussed the judgment roll of the British
Columbian court relating to the pro
bate of Dunsmuir's will and its rela
tion to the subsequent proceedings in
the San Francisco court. ,He declared
the record strange and wonderful, in
that the will had been probated In two
Jurisdictions without having been
sworn to by the attesting witnesses
in either court. He compared the pro
cedure to that of a man lifting himself
over the fence by his own boot p traps.
Judge Coyne was not present in court,
being confined to his hotel by illness.

Louis Monnier, 64 years old, was ar
rested yesterday afternoon by Police

charged with petty larceny. Monrier

went behind the altar rail and took
money left there by charitable persons
for the poor of the parish.

THE KNABE.
The Big San Francisco Dealers Cap
ture the Great Baltimore Piano
and Will Give it the Post,
tlon of Leaderâ-An
Important Move.
(Th* New York Music Trad* Review.)
"The Knabe piano will hereafter he
represented in San Francisco by ths
Wiley B. Allen Company. Ever since the
arrival of the Wiley B. Allen Company
in San Francisco they have proven a
piano distributing force of great power,
and the fact that they have been entrust
ed with the representation of this great
and honored piano demonstrates thai
this firm is one of ths most potent fig
ures in the piano trade of the West. It
is safe to prophesy concerning the new
representation, that never in the history
of the Knabe piano has it received on
the Pacific Coast the active handling
that it will receive at the hands of the
Wiley B. Allen Company, whose healths,
forceful methods have put them Into the
front rank of the retail piano forces in
this country. They are to be congratu
lated upon receiving the Knabe piano.
Its unassailable position makes it a value
of exceptional worth to the dealer, while
its artistic qualities emphasise the fact
that it is unique among the great pianos
of the musical world."

SULLIVAN OUT
OF PARK BOARD
Commissioner Bids Official
Farewell to Confreres and
Expresses His Well-Wishes
MONEY FOR NEW TRACK
Committee From Amateur
Athletic Driving Asso
" ciation Tenders Check
Park Commissioner Frank J. Sullivan
yesterday afternoon attended the meet
ing of the Park Commission in an of
ficial capacity for the last time. His
term has expired and Mayor Schmitz
will appoint a successor by the first of
the week. Before the close of the meet
ing Mr. Sullivan spoke of the pleasant
relations that had existed between the
members of the board and himself. On
occasions Mr. Sullivan had crossed
the sword of satire with Commissioner
Lloyd, and the passage of words has
done much toward livening up the rou
tine business of the board. But Com
missioner Lloyd says that Commission
er Sullivan is a rattling good fellow
and a good city official, and he is will
ing *to let bygones be bygones.
"Didn't I save the Park Commission
a neat sum per year in the Park News
contract?" asked the retiring official.
"That's what you did," answered
Lloyd, and President Spreckels added,
"And that reminds me that we ought
to give the Commissioner a vote of
thanks for his good work." The vote
of thanks was forthwith cast.
There were present at the meeting all
of the Commissioners âMessrs. Dingee,
Lloyd, Cummings and Sullivan and
President Adolph Spreckels In the
chair.
Superintendent McLaren made a re
port of work done during December in
Golden Gate and other city parks, and
said that he was getting ready for
work on the new athletic track. He
would require twelve horses of about
1500 pounds weight, and the commis
sion will advertise for bids for them.
Scrapers and other implements will be
needed. Their purchase was authorized.
GIFT ACKNOWLEDGED.
The reports of the Japanese tea gar
den and the children's playground
showed an excess of expenditures over
receipts during December, but the
amounts of the deficits were not great
er than usual.
Ths. offer of Nat Raphael to permit
the park teams to haul away clay from
a lot owned by him on Second street,
near Harrison, was declined, as the dis
tance of the lot from the park is too
great.
The Atlantic, Gulf and Pacific Com
pany was given permission to maintain
a blacksmith shop near the Olympic
salt water pier during the work of re
pairing.
The Smithsonian Institution, by Frank
Baker, superintendent, wrote to thank
the commission for the five large pho
tographs of Golden Gate Park present
ed to the national collection.
Commissicner Lloyd submitted copies
of the contract he had drawn up for
the board and Lyons & Co., successful
bidders for the Park News privilege.
The application of Quirk Brothers for
a return of $155, the amount paid for
one month's privilege of publishing the
News, was denied. The board received
a communication from Aaron Altmann
thanking it for greens from the park
for the decoration of the banquet hall
at the California Hotel, in which Mr.
Altmann was recently married.
A committee of four from the Ama
teur Athletic Driving Association, com
prised of Messrs. Frank O'Kane, W.
J. Simpson, I. L Borden and T. J.
Douglas, appeared before the board to
tender a check for $20,000, collected by
the association on behalf of the new
-athletic track, Golden Gate Park. All
of the money promised by the associa
tion has not yet been collected, but to
show good faith and confidence In the
project the association advanced part
of the sum.
QUESTION OF TRACK.
Mr. Borden said he thought that
horsemen would prefer a half mile
track to a three-quarter mile one.
President Spreckels thought that the
three-quarter mile track was preferable
and Mr. Borden added that while the
longer course might not suit all horse
j men they would nevertheless be satls
l fled with the track as planned because
! the automobile, bicycle, polo and other
I sports would be better provided for on
a three-quarter stretch.
The Police Department, at the re
quest of President Spreckels, made re
port that Policeman Kessing had ar
rested R. H. Trout for driving an auto
mobile beyond the speed limit. The
number on the machine did not cor
respond with Trout's permit. Trout
told President Spreckels that the
change of numbers was due to his ma
i chine having broken down and the
number on it was then substituted. It
transpired, after investigation, that
I Trout was running his auto too fast
I and he will be cited to appear before
the commissioners and show cause why
his permit should not be revoked.
Complaint was received from resi
dents near Lafayette square of certain
drifting sand. They do not know
whether the sand is on street or park
property. The matter was referred to
the City and County Attorney for in
vestigation. The squatters on Frank
lin square, Sixteenth and Bryant
streets, will be proceeded against by
the City Attorney.
The contract for furnishing 1200 cubic
yards of clay for the park was award
ed to Owen McHugh for 78 cents per
cubic yard.
The United States Life Saving Sta
tion at the beach has been using park
water and the Government has been
notified that a charge for same will
be made. Sheehan's place at the beach
has paid $80 for water taken during
eight months from park pipes without
the consent of the commission.
