HEACOCK WILL
DETAIN LEE
Expresses Belief That Women
Identifying Accused Could
Not Have Been Misled
REPUTATION IS GOOD
Detective Gibson Says It
Was Defendant's Brother
He Arrested for Crime
During the course of the hearing
yesterday of the case of the United
States against Le Roy Lee, Commis
sioner Heacock intimated that he
would hold the accused to answer be
fore the Grand Jury, Judge Heacock
said:
The defendant himself has testified that he
was In Mrs. Vallade's delicatessen store daily,
or nearly every day. for two months. His
features are very marked, and seeing him
even once you would know him again. It 1Â»
beyond the range vi probability that he is
not tbe Derson whom Mrs. Vallade and her
daughter have identified as the man who pass
ed a raised one-dollar stiver certlflcato on them
on IVivmber 20. They have positively iden
tified him as the man who waa there on Mon
day evening and on Friday evening. From
the testimony before me I have no doubts as
to his Identity.
The remarks quoted were incited by
the request of R. N. Frick, of counsel
for Lee, for an adjournment until
this morning so that he might procure
the attendance of a witness who
would swear that a man resembling
the defendant, but not he, had passed
at a bakery on the same street a $1
bill raised to a $20 bill. Judge Hea
cock granted the request for an ad
journment until 10 o'clock this morn
ing and then made the remarks Just
quoted.*
Lee produced J. M, Schuler, a mer
chant; F. J. King Jr., a butcher, and
James F. Lodge, an attorney, all of
Siskiyou County, who testified that
Lee's reputation and character for
truth and honesty were of the best.
John M. Featherston, secretary for
Redington & Co., whosale druggists,
gave similar testimony.
Police Detective E. L. Gibson swore
that he had been under the impres
sion that he had arrested the defend
ant twenty years ago for forgery. On
further investigation he said he found
that it was Lee's brother, Lyman Lee,
whom he had arrested on that occa
sion.
The defendant went on the stand to
correct a statement he had made un
der oath on the preceding day when
he said that he had never been ar
rested before. He said that he had
forgotten at the â¢time his arrest sev
eral years ago for having deer meat
in his possession during the close sea
son while camping. He had been ac
quitted on that charee.
SEPARATED FROM WIFE,
BOYD KILLS HIMSELF
Lnundry Worker Becomes Despondent
Because of Domestic Infelicity
and Turns on the Gas.
Tilford F. Boyd, a laundry employe,
killed himself last Thursday night by
inhaling illuminating gas in his room
at 1525% Mission street. Mrs. E. Ham
mersmith, the landlady, traced the
odor of gas to his room at 8:16 o'clock
yesterday morning. The door was
locked, but an entrance was effected
through a window and Boyd was
found alive, but was unconscious. Gas
was flowing from an open burner. He
died in the ambulance on the way
to the Central Emergency Hospital.
Boyd's wife left him some time "ago
and took their two children to Los
Angeles. Since then he had been very
despondent. He was 36 years old and
a native of Illinois.

CREEK WATERS
ARE AN ISSUE
Howard and Tevis Exchange
Letters and Promise to
Back Up Claims to Title
DISPUTE STARTS WARM
Ownership of Streams That
Are in Two Counties Is
the Bone of Contention
A sharp controversy has arisen be
tween the Spring Valley Water Com
pany and the Bay Cities Water Com
pany concerning the ownership of tha
waters of lsabelle Creek and Arroyo
Valle Creek in Santa Clara and Ala
meda counties. The Spring Valley
Water Company has notified the Bay
Cities people In the following letter that
it owns and has tha right to all tha
flow of the waters in the two creeks
and that it will enforce its ownership:
Gentlemen: We are informed that you are
considering or contemplating th* construc
tion ot works to divert the waters or a portion
of the waters of lsabelle Creek and Arroyo
Valle Creek. In Santa Clara County and in
Alameda County, from their own channels.
We beg to inform you. at this earliest mo
ment, that this company owns and has the
right to the flow of all the waters flowing and
to flow In both said creeks, and will enforce
such ownership and right at all times. Very
respectfully yours
SPRING VALLEY WATER COMPANY,
By Charles Webb Howard. President.
On the other hand the Bay Cities
Water Company notifies the Spring Val
ley that it'will use all the flood waters
of the two streams and will defend ita
title. The response to tha Spring Val
ley Is as follows:
Gentlemen: We have yours of to-day advis
ing us that you claim all tbe water* ef Isabel
Creek and Arroyo Valle Creak. In reply we
bee to Inform you that we hare legally ac
quired, now own and intend to us* all of the
flood waters of these streams above our points
of intended diversion and that we are pre
pared to defend our title thereto. Respectfully,
BAT CITIES WATER COMPANY,
By William S. Tevis. President.

CITY GOLFERS ELECT OFFICERS
California Women to Form a New Association
The annual meeting of the members
of the San Francisco Golf and Country
Club will be held in the clubhouse ad
jacent to the Presidio reservation this
afternoon, beginning at 4 o'clock. The
principal matters to come before the
members are the reports of the officers
for the past year, the election of of
ficers to serve during the coming twelve
months and the ratification of certain
changes in the by-laws.
The nominating committee, consisting
of Rolla V. Watt, George E. Starr and
S. C. Buckbee. has made the following
nominations for the council: Captain,
J. S. Oyster. R. D. Girvin, R. J. Woods,
Dr. J. R. Clark and S. L. Abbot Jr.
These nominees will be elected and at
their first meeting after election will
chbose a president, vice president and
secretary-treasurer. The president will
appoint tournament, house and other
committees. Captain J. S. Oyster, R. J.
Woods, R. D. Girvin and Dr. J. R.
Clark are members of the present coun
< 11, R. D. Girvin having taken the place
of F. H. Beaver, who resigned after be
ing elected at the last annual meeting.

DUCK SHOOTING SEASON IS AT ITS HEIGHT
The storm of last week scattered the
ducks and thus impaired the New
Year's day shooting on the marshes
about the bay. The scattering of the
birds is considered only temporary and
good sport is expected while the
weather remains cold and clear.
The season is at its height just now.
One indication of this is fbund in the
fact that the ammunition houses can
hardly turn out shells in sufficient
numbers to meet the demand.
There are but few canvasbacks in
the market, although they are in the
bay in great numbers. There were but
three of these birds in sixty sacks of
ducks received in the markets in one
day recently.
Otto Feudner and Mr. Hoffman had
a good shoot at San Pablo last Sunday
and Monday, securing eighty-four cans.

Copies of the proposed changes in the

for signature, and if the requisite num
ber of signatures are received the
amended by-laws will be adopted.
On Tuesday morning next, beginning
promptly at 9:30 o'clock, the ladies of
the San Francisco Golf and Country
Club will hold competitions in driving
and approach-putting for prizes.
The promoters of the proposed Cali
fornia Women Golfers' Association sug
gest that one woman of each club In
the association be asked to serve as
captain of her own club. She will then
be notified by the secretary of the as
sociation before each tournament of
the place, hour and day of the next
event, and will send to the secretary
the names of those members of her
club who will play.
Except urging as many as possible to
play, she will have no duties to per
form until her club holds the tourna
ment, which, with the helo of other
captains present, she will be expected
to superintend, taking care that caddies
are on hand, score cards ready and
The birds were in excellent condition.

One hundred and four fine birds were
bagged, the majority of which >vere

of this club are on overflowed land In
side the levee and the ducks from there
are pronounced by the persons fortu
nate enough to receive them, the finest
game birds that are brought to this
city.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1905.
Entries for the competition for the
California Association Football cup
must be made not later than the 15th
inst. with Henry Roberts, secretary of
the union. The entrance fee of $2 50
must be paid at the time the entry is
made. The officers of the California
Association Football Union will take
full charge of the contest, one match
being played each Sunday until the
contest is finished. The cup series will
not begin until the programme for the
champiohship of the league is played
out. At the end of the cup series the
clubs will share in the net gate receipts
in proportion to the number of games
played by each team.
During the New Year's day vacation
four matches of association football
were played in this vicinity. On Sun
day a scratch team of the Vampires
played a tie game with the Occidentals
on the Alameda ground. The Pickwick
team beat the Eagles two goals to
nothing at Idora Park, Oakland. On
Monday a scratch team of the Vam
pires, containing five or six members
of the first eleven, beat the Pickwicks
two goals to nothing. On the same
day the Oakland Hornets played a
friendly match against a team repre
senting the Seamen's Institute at Idora
Park, Oakland-
luncheon provided. The captain will be
notified of the number that will be
present, each visitor paying for her
own luncheon and bringing, if she
wishes, her own caddie. The captain
must see that a large card for the
names of the contestants and their
scores is on the bulletin board of the
club. The score card must be signed by
the captain and sent by mail to the
secretary of the association after the
tournament. When the executive com
mittee of the association wishes to com
municate with any club it will be done
through the captain, who will be re
garded as the representative of the
members. ' The captain should consider
It her duty to speak well of the asso
ciation and to try to keep up the inter
est of her fellow members in golf. The
women members of all the clubs In the
association will be expected to play in
as many tournaments as possible and
to encourage others to do the same
Tournaments will be held on the course
of each club about once a year, ac
cording to the number of clubs in the
association. The only expenses that
contestants will incuT will be fares,
luncheons and caddies.

CROWDS CHEER
OLD CHAMPION
Give Greyhound Pocatelli
a Hearty Reception When
He Appears on the Field
That the public dearly loves a cham
pion was demonstrated last Sunday at
Union Coursing Park. Pocatelli, the
great greyhound, which has been in re
tirement for several months, was sent
out as a relief dog during one of the
trials to get a little running. The spec
tators recognized the handsome hound
at once and cheered him as he showed
all his old-time cleverness and much of
his former speed.
The up-country greyhound is entered
in the stake to be run to-morrow. He
meets Forest Fire in his first course,
which he should win handily. After
that the competition will grow warmer
as each succeeding round is run, and it
will need all his former speed to land
him the ultimate winner.
P. Doyle has decided to retire Lib
erator, which coursing men have called
the "iron dog," from the field. He will
be placed in the stud and much is ex
pected of him there. Liberator is by
Fear Not-Lady Thorndyke, and has
been noted for his cleverness and for
his great stamina, coupled with aver
age speed. He won many stakes and
has â¢ seldom been outside the money.
Week after week he has been in the
thick of the competition and has al
ways rendered a good account of him
self.
A match race and an open stake will
make up to-morrow's card at Union
Park. Humboldt and Carlow Boy met
last week in the deciding course of a
stake and a close race resulted. The
match was then decided upon. The
likely winners look to be:
Match raceâHumboldt to beat Carlow Boy.
Open stake, fifty-six entriesâBob R. Story's
Sister, Renerade Apache. Lost Chord, Anna
Ryne, Texas JHI. Melllnwood, Belle Barle,
Cranberry Sauce. Sunnybrook, Qulta, Medea,
Pocatelli. Jlgamaroo. Little Mercy, Mickey
Free, Belle Free, Rocked Asleep, Glaucus,
Reckless Acrobat. McVey, Richard Anton,
Hidden Hatred, Raby Aurora, Texas Mamie,
lone Hill,. Wild Turkey and McHenry.
moving. Charles McDonald also shot
at Sears Point and secured twenty-one
birds.
The shooting on the Empire Gun Club
preserve this season has been the best
in the history of the club, English
snipe, ducks and quail being plentiful
on the Monterey preserve. Considerable
annoyance has been caused recently by
poachers, and the club has decided to
no longer tolerate this nuisance. It has
set aside a fund for the prosecution of
all intruders. The keeper's force will be
doubled and a keen watch will be.kept
for all offenders against the law. They
will be prosecuted to the bitter end. The
club's trapshooting grounds will be
opened at Alameda Point on Sunday,
March 12, and will remain open there
after on the second Sunday of each
month" until the trapshooting season
ends.

ONE OF H. W. KELLER'S CANDI
DATES FOR HONORS IN ALL
AGE STAKE AT BAKERSFIELD.
FIELD TRIALS
ARE PROMISING
Many Sportsmen Will Go to
Bakersfield to See the
Hunting Dogs in Action
Everything is in readiness for the
twenty-second annua! meet of the Pa
cific Coast Field Trials Club, which
will open on the Haggin and Tevis
property near* Bakersfield on Monday.
The handlers of the high class setters
and pointers entered for the trials re
port the conditions as good. The rain
came at an opportune time, one inch
falling last Friday and helping matters
greatly.
Many of the well known sportsmen
who enjoy seeing the work in the field
of these intelligent dogs, will leave for
the scene to-day. This will give them
an opportunity for a shoot to-morrow
c-n quail or ducks. Those who cannot
get away to-day will go down to-mor
row morning, arriving in time to wit
ness the draw.
H. W. Keller, the Santa Monica
sportsman, will have several represen
tatives in the trials. One of his favor
ites will be Sombro, which will be han
dled by W. B. Coutts in the all-age
stake. She finished third in the mem
bers' stake last year and divided third
with Keepsake in the derby. She was
bred by W. W. Van Arsdale and is
by Llewellin Drake-Shadow.
She was sent East last year, but like
all California-bred field dogs she was
not familiar with prairie chickens and
did not do well. The California dogs
are disciplined for hunting barn-yard
fowl and they associate the prairie
chickens for these.
The Eastern Field Trials Club is the
only organization of this character
which is older than the California club,
having been organized some two years
in advance of it.
ARTHUR WILD ARRESTED
UNDER STRONG SUSPICION
Is Suspected of Being a Confederate
of Gang of Clothing
Thieves.
Arthur Wild was arrested yesterday
by Detectives Regan and O'Connell
of the Southern Police Station as one
of a pair of men who have been steal
ing on a large scale from clothing
stores in the district south of Market
street.
The police say that Wild worked
with a confederate and that the two
have stolen seventy-eight pairs of
trousers, as well as other clothing dur
ing the last week. Wild was arrested
while attempting to dispose of sixteen
pairs of trousers in a second-hand
store at Sixth nvd Howard streets. It
was the practice of the two to enter
a store and while one fitted on a suit
of clothes, under the pretense of mak
ing a purchase, the other dexterously
filled a grip.'*
Wild is a man about 25 years of
age, well dressed and of smooth man
ners.
Some men must get tight before they
can let themselves loose.
PLAYERS REST
FOR A SPELL
No Tennis Tournaments
âºScheduled on California
Club or on Public Courts
There will be no tennis tournaments on
the Golden Gate Park or on the Califor
nia Club courts to-morrow. The mem
bers of the latter organization have en
gaged in many tournaments during the
last few weeks and they will be given
a rest for some time. Nothing is
scheduled at present except the con
tinuous tournament, which will move
more smoothly in future.
The players who frequent the park
courts' have had a long and much need
ed rest from tournament play. They
have not met in match play for about
two months, and every man in the park
is anxiously awaiting the first tourna
ment of the new year. This event is
set for the 15th inst. It will be a handi
cap singles tournament and it is ex
pected that there will be in the neigh
borhood of one hundred players. A sim
ilar event held about a year ago on
the public courts was won by Clarence
Griffin. One hundred and four players,
the largest number ever entered in a
tournament in this section of the coun
try, competed. As the juvenile club has
more than fifty members, all of whom
will probably participate, the entry list
should be even larger than it was a
year ago. Entries for the event close
on the 12th and should be sent to J. M.
Baker, secretary of the club, at Oak
and Stanyan streets.
With nothing else to take up the at
tention of the tennis players the con
tinuous tournament at the California
Club should prove a marked success.
Most of the players in the different
classes are evenly matched and the
rivalry between them is keen. There
will be a great struggle for the first
place. George Janes has gone to the
top and will be a hard man to dis
lodge. Collier almost turned the trick
last Saturday, when Janes beat him by
the narrowest of margins.
Collier Is improving;, and when he
meets Janes to-morrow the enthusiasts
look for a great match. Janes is also
Improving and in a short time should
leave Collier far behind.
At present the fight for first place
seems to be between Janes and Collier,
but there is anpther player who has
been overlooked. He is Dr. Bush, the
number four man. The doctor Joined
the club only a short time ago, but has
had considerable experience in the
East. He has a perfect build for ten
nis and also an excellent style. When
he becomes accustomed to the courts
and gets into his old-time form he
should have no difficulty getting to the
top. Last Monday he beat Harry Rolfe,
and will dispose of Cornell easily. This
will put him next to Collier. There will
be some excellent matches when Janes,
Collier and Bush come together.
Carl Gardner and Melville Long are
two others In the first class who will
work their way to the top. Long re
cently graduated from the second class
and is improving rapidly.
In the second class Harold Gabriel is
easily the best. He stands an excellent
chance of going into the first class.
George Cutter has worked his way out
of the third class, but met a stumbling
block in Paul Jones, the number three
man.
In the third class Albert Kenyon and
Moulten Warner are having no trouble
keeping at the top. Kenyon tried to go
up a class, but was beaten by Cutter
in straight sets.
In the fourth class Judge Troutt still
heads the list. He has not played as
yet, but will probably meet Knowlton
to-day. Tbe latter has made more
progress than any man in the tourna
ment. He has gone up eight numbers
TARS TALKING
OF ELECTIONS
San Francisco Yachtsmen
Meet Next Wednesday to
Choose Three Nominators
On Wednesday, the 13th inst., being
the second Wednesday in the month, a
meeting of the members of the San
Francisco Yacht Club will be held in
the banqueting-room of the California
Hotel, San Francisco, for the purpose
of electing a nominating committee of
three, who will choose the candidates
for the various offices. The candidates
selected by the nominating committee
make up the "regular tidket," the of
fices to be filled being those of com
modore, vice commodore, port captain,
recording secretary, financial secretary,
treasurer and measurer. Other nom
inations may be made for the various
offices.
The nominees will be balloted for at
the annual meeting, which will be held
on the second Wednesday in February.
year the nominees for vice com
modore, port captain, treasurer and
financial secretary were the same on
both the "regular" ticket and the
"members' " ticket, the contest taking
place for the offices of commodore, sec
retary and measurer. It is understood
W. G. Morrow, the present commodore,
is desirous of re-election, though the
past season, owing to the continued
opposition of a certain body of the
members, cannot have been altogether
pleasant. He is certain to be nominat
ed as a candidate for the office of com
modore, either on the "regular" ticket
or on a "members' " ticket.

It seemed impossible for a favorite
to get across the line in front at Oak
land yesterday, for in succession Nul
lah, Glendenning, Best Man, Bronze
Wing, Follow Me and Little Wally
went the toboggan route. Of the win
ning horses Sterling Towers, Harry
Beck and The Lady Rohesla were well
supported. The weather was fine and
a large crowd viewed the sport.
Bonner, the colored rider, took the
first event of the day with Jim Neil's
four-year-old Distributor, a SO to 1
shot. The gelding was in front from
the start and, standing a hard drive,
downed Urbano, another outsider, a
"hose. Nullah made a poor favorite,
losing the show to Mogregor. Stand
ard received some stable backing, but
did not make good.
GLENDENNING FAILS BACKERS.
Glendenning, which was Installed an
â¢odds on choice for the five furlong sell
ing scramble, made a dismal showing,
' failing to crowd in among the first
three. The speedy but faint-hearted
splinter never was partial to weight,
and 114 pounds anchored him as effec
â  tively as an Oregon boot would a San
Quentin boarder. Bell Reed, ridden by
Davis and 16 to 5 in the betting, early
passed the favorite, winning easily at
the wire from Revolt and Dora I, two
= dutsiders of the party.
â¢â¢ A bunch of ordinary platers started
in the third, a six furlong selling af
fair, for which Best Man was backed
â¢ down to 18 to 5 favoritism. The winner
turned up in Sterling Towers, a 6 to 1
shot, played to some extent. Kunz
rode the mare and in a hard drive with
Goldfinder and Troy, the trio finishing
necks apart, was awarded the verdict.
Best Man ran a close fourth, only a
neck farther away.
HARRY BECK A WINNER.
Harry Beck, one of the touted good
things for the mile selling number,
came through. The old gelding's odds
were pounded down from 10 to 5, and in
a hard drive with the favorite. Bronze
Wmg, finished first by a head in 1:42.
McÃrlde handled the winner to excel
lent advantage. Bard Burns cut out
the running to the stretch, where he
began backing up and was beaten for
â¢the show by Isabellita.
* Another favorite was laid away in
the mile and fifty-yard selling fixture.
On his previous form Follow Me looked
like a ripe one and went to the post
an 11 to 10 favorite. He had Sam
Greenfield on' his and, after
showing the way to the stretch, began
to droop and fade, finishing last. Scots
man, piloted by Davis, and second
choice in the ring, then outstayed
Metetersinger, winning by less than a

length. The show went to Dungannon.
Charley Henshall's The Lady Ro
hesia played merry pranks with her
field in the concluding six and a half
furlong purse run. â¢ Her price dropped
by decrees from 4 to 9 to 5 and the
mare galloped home five lengths In ad
vance of Padua and Peter J. Little
Wally, the favorite, met with some
interference at the start and was not
placed.
TRACK NOTES.
The Follansbee handicap will be
run to-day.
Lee Blasingame could not pick up
a purse at Oakland and will ship his
stable to Los Angeles, where the
game is easier. Steve Judge will also
send Brennus and one or two others
down to Ascot Park.
A. W. Daly took out a license yes
terday and from now on will train
the horses owned by his daughter,
Mrs. Cora Eckert. Rector and Circus
are two of the stable.
Kunz and W. Davis were the stars,
each piloting two winners.
The poolrooms in town Taid 20 to 1
against Harry Beck.
Best Man, which ran in the coiors
of Dr. Rowell, was formerly owned
by Lester Reiff.

Boxer Fitzgerald Wins on Points.
MILWAUKEE, Jan. 6.âWillie Fitz
gerald of Brooklyn gained the decision
on points over Maurice Thompson of
Butte to-night at the end of a six
round bout before the Badger Athletic
Club. The fight was fast from start to
finish. Young O'Leary of Milwaukee
won from Carl Walters of Chicago In
six rounds.
Boer Wrestler Is Defeated.
CHICAGO, Jan. 6.âJohn J. Rooney
of Chicago defeated Captain Vissier,
the Boer wrestler, in a mixed match
here to-night. The match was at two
styles, catch-as-catch-can and Graeco-
Roman. Vissier won the first fall,
Graeco-Roman, and Rooney the second
and third, catch-as-catch-can

MARKSMEN WIN HONORS
WITH THE REVOLVER
Oakland Revolver Club Holds Second
Annual Prize Shooting at
Indoor Range.
OAKLAND, Jan. 6.âThe members
of the Oakland Revolver Club have
Just concluded their second annual
prize tournament, which was held at
the club's indoor range, 412 Four
teenth street. The tournament was a
success in every way, being attended
by nearly fifty pistol and revolver en
thusiasts from Oakland and San Fran
cisco. The two best five shot scores
counted, the highest possible score be
ing 50. The prizes will be distributed
to the winners to-morrow evening at
which time another prize shoot will be
held.

their high scores:

Action on Berger Case Postponed.
The directors of the Olympic Club
were not ready to decide the Berger
case last night, so action was deferred
for two weeks.
Miller Pleads Guilty.
George Miller pleaded guilty to a
charge of robbery in Judge Lawlor's
court yesterday and will be sentenced
to-day. As there are five prior con
victions against him it is likely that
he will get life imprisonment. On No
vember 20 he held up Richard C.
Malone at Filbert and Fillmore streets,
relieving him of $78 45. He was
caught in the act by Policeman John
S. O'Neil and after an exchange of
shots Miller was captured.

FLEET PONIES TO RACE
ON TRACK AT SAN MATEO
Undersized Thoroughbreds Will Carry the Colors
of Well-Known Sportsmen in Sprint Races.
The second day's racing of the Cali
fornia Polo and Pony Racing Associa
tion will be held this afternoon at San
Mateo on the private track of Charles
W. Clark. There are six races on the
card and all the great ponies will start,
including Fusilade, Becky, Bas Blancs,
Silver Dick, Marigold and others.
C. W. Clark will start his clever pony
Bas Blancs in the handicap at Aye fur
longs. She is a bay mare by Bliss
Rucker, a thoroughbred, and won the
champion stakes last Monday cleverly,
running the six furlongs In 1:21 Vi, with
Charley Dunphy and 150 pounds In the
saddle. This is the association record
foÂ£ the distance.
The fields will all be large and with
such clever gentlemen riders up as R.
M. Tobin, Frank Skinner, Charley Dun
phy, R. J. Dunphy, W. Dieckman, Paul
Clagstone, E. J. Coleman, F. J. Carolan
and others, exciting sport is assured.

WOMEN MAY WITNESS
THE WRESTLING MATCH
Will Be Admitted Free to Jenkins-
Carkcek Bout When Accom
panied by Escorts.
The number of women who attended
the last wrestling match given under
the management of the Hayes Valley
Club induced Manager Levy to try a
new departure for next Tuesday's bout
between Jack Carkeek and Tom Jen
kins. The fair Â»sex when accompanied
by escorts will be admitted free and the
entire balcony will be reserved for
them. Manager Levy believes there is
no good reason why wrestling should
not become popular with the gentler
sex, which is tabooed from boxing
matches for obvious reasons.
These are busy days in the giants'
training quarters. Carkeek is working
harder than at any previous time for
years in anticipation of a severe bout
fronrKthe rolling mill man. He comes
of a race of natural giants and is never
far from condition, but he is taking no
chances. Jenkins also has a profound
respect for his opponent and is going
through a consistent course of training
at San Rafael.
Considerable interest has been
aroused by the announcement that
George Braun and Al Brill of the Cali
fornia Cycling Club will wrestle a pre
liminary to the big show. Braun is con
sidered unbeatable at his weight, but
he will meet a wiry citizen in Brill.
Radcliffe's Sentence Postponed.
Harry Radcliffe, convicted of mur
der in the second degree, appeared for
sentence in Judge Cook's court yester
day. His attorney made a motion for
a new trial on numerous grounds, in
cluding nearly forty alleged errors of
the Judge and District Attorney. The
Judge ordered that briefs be submitted
by each side on the points raised and
postponed sentence till February 17.
Radcliffe shot and killed Andrew Riley
at Ninth and Sheridan streets on the
morning of June 26.
Life Sentence for a Murderer.
RISING SUJJ, Ind., Jan. 6.âJames
Gillespie, convicted by a jury yester
day of the murder of his twin sister,
Elizabeth, to-day was sentenced to the
penitentiary for life. A re-trial was
denied, but an appeal was granted.

sons in Barring the Horses of

LONDON, Jan. 6.âSecretary Weath
erby of the Jockey Club declines to
discuss the reason for the action of the
. Jockey Club in interdicting Richard
Croker from training his horses on
Newmarket Heath, pointing out that
under the Jockey Club ruies it is not
necessary for the stewards to give
reasons for their actions. Mr. Croker
1 wired to the Associated Press from
\ Dublin to-night as follows:
"Have not the slightest idea why the
â  Jockey Club barred my horses from
Newmarket."
} It transpires that Mr. Croker's train-
I er, J. E. Bremer, was summoned to
attend the Jockey Club offices, where
Secretary Weatherby verhally intimat
ed that the stewards would not allow
Mr. Croker's horses to be trained at
Newmarket. In reply to a question
Secretary Weatherby added there was
nothing personally against Bremer.
Curiously, the entries of Mr. Croker's
horses for the forthcoming races have
been accepted by Secretary Weatherby
recently.
In an interview at Dublin to-night
Mr. Croker repeated that he was not
aware of any act on his part which
could have provoked the interdiction.
He said he had no idea what course the
Irish Tu>f Club would take on the
I forty horses he had in Ireland. He had
1 no hesitancy In Baying he took greater
pleasure racing in Ireland than in
either England or America

MATCH STILL
HANGING FIRE
Murphy Will Not Meet Neil.
So Greggains Is Trying
to King In Dick Hyland
Frankie Neil, former bantam cham
pion, is about matched to put up his
dukes against Dick Hyland. There is
a slight difference in weight, but that
will probably be patched up shortly.
The Neil-Murphy go is off, as Murphy
wired Aiex Greggains last night that
he had a bout on for the 25th inst. and
could not come.
As a preliminary to the main event,
Greggains is trying to sign Sam Berger
and Jim Casey. It is understood Ber
ger has been clamoring for a chance
to redeem himself and the promoter
of the. San Francisco Club Is doing his
best to accommodate him. Casey was
on hand Jast night with $500 to bet on
himself, put all efforts to locate Ber
ger proved futile and the quest was
given up for the time being.
Sam McClintic, who is managing Hy
iand, insists that the weight be 122
pounds at 6 o'clock, while Neil will
hear nothing but 122 pounds ringside.
They wrangled and argued last night
for many hours over this point and
neither would concede a bit. It is more
than likely Neil will win out, for he is
the drawing card and therefore the
one who is most likely to do the dic
tating.
A little side play in which Casey and
Jack Johnson figured created quite a
ripple of fun while it lasted. Casey
was sitting in one corner of the room
and Johnson in another. Casey leaned
over to a friend and with inflated chest
remarked: "I would like to go on wid
that black man." Thi3 bit of informa
tion was quickly conveyed to Mr. John
son, who immediately struck a pictur
esque pose and in his gentle way said,
"Wal. yo just clean out dat backvroom
and Aw will fight Mistah Casey for foh
dollahs. Aw am in dls business for de
money and Aw don't care whether It
am big or little."
The match could not be arranged last
night as the janitor was not present,
so the stars had to content themselves
with casting a few ferocious glances
at each other from their strongholds.
WOULD HOLD MORRIS LEVY
RESPONSIBLE FOR DEBT
Judgment Creditor Seeks to Recover
Money Voluntarily Given to Buz
ayel, the Colored Wrestler.
Morris Levy, manager of the Hayes
Valh y Athletic Club, was up before
Justice of the Peace Daniels yesterday
on an order of examination. The order
is the result of a judgment of $80 ob
tained by E. J. Lapachet against' Vi
ctor Ajax, sometimes known as John
Batta and who as Buzayel wrestled with
Jenkins recently. Lapachet's process
server was present when Levy handed
Buzayel $100. As Levy was about to
give the colored wrestler another $80
the writ of garnishment is alleged to
have been served. Levy testified that
Buzayel had fouled his opponent and
on that account no money was coming
to him, he having forfeited his rights
in the case. Levy said he had present
ed Buzayel with $100 as a voluntary
gift and would have given him $80
more under the same condition had the
process server not intervened. Judge
Daniels said he could do nothing un
der the code and advised that a new
civil action against the Hayes Valley
Club was the only way to settle the
dispute.

LIBRARY OF RARE MUSICAL
WORKS LEFT BY THOMAS
CHICAGO, Jan. 6.âThe announce
ment of a public bequest of Theodore
Thomas' unique musical libraryâa rare
collection of scores and manuscripts
from the famous masters âis to be
made within a month. During his en
tire career, the leader collected scores
and manuscripts, many of which were
presented to him by the authors. The
library is valued at 2300,000. Every
year he added to it, with the result
that it is the most valuable and ex
tensive private collection of its kind
in America.
Some of the original scores of Wag
ner, Liszt and other composers, it is
said, will be presented to the public
library.
The Chicago Orchestra, although the
formal announcement of the disposal
of the gift is withheld until a later
season, probably will receive the bulk
of the bequest.
The funeral of Theodore Thomas took
place in St. James Episcopal Church
to-day with simple rites. The Chicago
Orchestra attended as an organization
at the church and afterward formed
part of the cortege to the receiving
vault at Graceland Cemetery, where
the body will be kept until forwarded
for interment to New York.

VAINLY TRIES
TO END LIFE
Drink Crazed Man Leaps
Twice From High Building
and Escapes Without Hurts
DAZED BUT UNIXJUKED
Inebriate Wanders About
the Streets After Attempts
at Suicide Prove Futile
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Jan. 6.âFollowing a pro
j tracted spree James Carruthers this
morning decided to commit suicide.
Mounting to the top of a four-story
building occupied by the California
laundry, Carruthers removed his coat
and hat and stepped off. He expected
to land on the pavement, four stories
below, with bones broken and body
. shattered, but he had leaped from the
! wrong side of the building. He landed
|on the roof of a saloon only one story
i below.
! This preliminary dash jarred him
j considerably, but he was still deter
! mined to throw himself to destruction,
j and again he leaped from the roof,
i This time he landed astride of two elec-
I trie light wires, which he caught with
! arms and legs, thus preventing himself
j from falling thirty feet below to the
! cement sidewalk.
Fear of electric currents caused Car-
I ruthers to scramble upon a projecting
cornice, from which he leaped to the
| porch of a saloon and from there to the
â  ground. After reaching terra flrma
! without hat, coat or â vsast, Carruthers
i started wandering about the streets ap-
J parently but otherwise untn
! jured by his several leaps toward
j death.
ARCHIBISHOP ANSWERS
AMERICAN CHURCH PEOPLE
Objection to the Educational Tax, He
Says, Is Not Understood
Over Here.
LONDON, Jan. 6.âThe Archbishop
of Canterbury, replying to a memorial
from the Baptists and Presbyterians of
America, asking his assistance to pre
vent the hardships suffered by non
conformists of Great Britain, who ob
ject conscientiously to the payment of
the educational tax. incloses a copy of
a previous reply on the same subject
to a memorial from the Baptist con
vention in New York.
This reply contrasts the Bishop of
Canterbury's difficulty of understand
ing the present situation in connection
with the presidential election in Amer
ica with the difficulty the American
citizen might experience in under
standing the complex educational
problems of Great Britain and main
tains that the act under which the
tax is levied does not interfere in the
smallest degree with religious liberty
and that the whole tendency of the
bill is to remove so called non-con
formist differences. He says the
present controversy in Great Britain
would be found to "have other origin
and interests than those purely edu
cational."
The Archbishop in a letter inclosing
the reply says it has afforded him
great pleasure to help cement the
friendship, which he hopes will con
tinue, between members of the Church
of England and their Christian breth
ren of America, and continues:
"You may rely on my doing every
thing in my power, not only in Eng
land, but throughout the world, to re
move any mischievous causes of mis
understanding among Christian peo
ple."
WOULD PUNISH FATHERS
WHO DESERT FAMILIES
Bill Providing a Ceil for Cruel Parents
to Be Inrtdouced in â¢
Washington.
TACOMA, Jan. 6.âPolice Justice Wil
liams of Bellingham is framing a bill
which will go before the next Legisla
ture with the support of a majority of
the Superior Court Judges and Police
Justices of this State. It aims to pre
vent the wholesale desertion of their
families by men who sometimes find
it easier to run away and support
themselves instead of properly caring
for their wives and children. The bill
will provide for the punishment by im
prisonment or fine, or both, of hus
bands or wives who desert or abandon
their families.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
AVcc York Stocks lower. Cotton higher.
Local Stock Market fairly active, mining shares especially.

C< TTONâThe Journal of Commerce this

He gives his views on the cotton situation.
They are diametrically opposite to the opinions
f-Xpressed yesterday by W. C. Lovering, and
-he cotton oil authority maintains that as a
result of the sale of 7,000,000 bales on a 10c
basis the farmers are in a position to take
care of the surplus amount ot cotton and that
' spinners, having bought largely at the higher
â¢level, will be more disposed to support t!i<Â»
market than to work against It.

New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Jar. 6.âGathering disappoint
ment in view of the moderate investment de
mand for stocks and over the reluctance of
the outside public to come into the market
caused a general decline in prices to-day. Tha
growing sluggishness of the market and the
difficulty of getting prices up has proved irk
some all the week to the room-trade element,
. whose livelihood is dependent on motion in
the market of one kind or another. The
obstacles to the advance prompted a consid
erable proportion of this element to sell stocks.
' It did not appear there was any great amount
<5f liquidation in the movement and there waa
no new development to prompt a seHlng of
securities. Doubt over the level to which
prices have been lifted and over their stability
at that level may have prompted some selling
The movement was not widely different from
â¢ that on preceding days except that there was
a lack of such sustaining points In the market
as had been used up to to-day. With the
r. exception of a momentary' advance in Amalga
mated Copper and some continued absorption
t>f the Briea and Reading there was no strong
feature at any time to-day. The rise in
' Amalgamated Copper was connected with dis
cussion of its dividend prospects. As to the
effects of the decline It became fairly general
â¢ in the course of the day, thus indicating a
profit-taking character for the movement. The
news of the day did not change from the
recent favorable tenor as affecting property
values. Some effect may have been produced
' by the report of the definite determination to
appeal the Northern Securities decision, whioh
caused a drop in that stock on the curb. But
Its subsequent rally did not help the stock
â¢market. Railroads reporting earnings made
no deviation from the recent advancing ten
dency. In the cotton market the decline in
prices was reported to be attracting at least
large buying orders from foreign spinners and
the week's exports of corn were regarded as
â¢ highly gratifying. The shipments of the lat
ter cereal for the week rose to the largest for
any week in a long time. This revival in
the corn movement Is of special Importance on
account of the languid outward movement of
wheat and promises an effective supply In
the foreign exchange market. That market
'' continued to advance to-day and Inquiries by
foreign bankers showed that there is no abate
ment in the prospects for the outward move
' ment of gold. It is evident, however, that
th'! movement of currency has turned in full
tide from the interior to New York, the pre
liminary figures Indicating a gain by the banks
of more than $6,000,000. There is a feeling
growing up that a large accumulation of re
serves at thla center is now awaited aa an
. â¢ effective opportunity for the offering of large
new issues of securities. The fall in prices
gained its greatest velocity in the final hour
' and overran Itself resulting in an active de
mand to cover shorts by the room-traders.
â¢ The closing was irregular.
The bond market was strong, but the move
ment continued irregular. Total sales, pa*
. value, $6,326.080.
United States bonds were all unchanged on
the last call.

Future Grain and Provisions.
CHICAGO. Jan. 6.âNotwithstanding in
creased shipments from Argentina, the wheat
market here opened with a fair degree of
firmness, May being up %Â©%c at $1 14%Â©
1 14%. The strengthening feature was the
continued light movement in the Northwest.
Although a comparison of receipts to-day with
those of the corresponding day a year ago did
not show a sufficient decrease to create bull
ishness, the figures were enough to keep alive
some hope in the bull traders. Business, how
ever, wss much restricted during ths early
part of the session, pit traders being bearlshly
inclined and waiting to avail themselves of a
possible slump. Weakness of coarse grains
brought out some profit-taking in wheat by
local longs Offerings were not readily taken
and in consequence prices eased off a trifle.
May declining to $1 14%. Later in the day a
firmer tone developed, mainly as a result ot
reports of Improved demand for cash wheat
at all the principal center* A factor that
helped to Increase bullish sentiment was Brad
street's report showing exports of wheat and
flour of 1,412,000 bushels for the week.
The high folnt on May for ths day was
reached at fSIS%. The market closed strong
with May af $1 15.

ited much weakness, being subjected to re
newed selling by several commission houses

subsequently regained by short covering. May

Wheat and Other Grains.
WHEATâThere was no foreign news wor
thy of note. Chicago was several ?: actions
higher. The Modern Miller aayn that satis
factory reborta sre being received concern
ing the condition of the winter Wheat crop.
Advices indicate that the top growth is small
but healthy and the root growth is vigorous
and of an unusual length. Weather has been
quite favorable since winter set in and the
setback received hy the plant earlier in the
season on account of lack of moisture has been
fully recovered.
In this market futures were higher, but the
cash grain was quiet and unchanged.

Family Retail Market,
Fresh Ranch Eggs are cheaper and they will
probably be even lower before long, as this is
the time of the year when the production in
creases and prices have a downward tendency.
Butter is more plentiful in the wholesale mar
ket, but retail rates have not been affected
as yet.

week and there are plenty of Apples and Or
anges to be had. The markets are slenderly
supplied with garden vegetables and several
kinds are higher. %

the old quotations and, aside from an advance

Poultry and Game.
Under continued light receipts and a steady
demand the market for live Poultry was in
good shape and available supplies cleaned up
quickly at the previously quoted rates. Dressed
Turkeys were firm at a narrower range of
prices. Thirty-Aye cases came in. Game was

and Bonus.

snd Bond Exchange, particularly In bonds, but
aside from a decline in Gas and Electric to 361
there were no fluctuations of note. There
were some sugar sales at steady prices.
The oil stocks continued dull and the Tono
pah mining shares were also comparatively
quiet. There was active trading In the Corn
stock mining shares without any remarkable
fluctuations.

LIVELY FIGHT
AT A BREWERY
Attorney Joseph F. Coffey
Claims He Was Choked and
Beaten by Father and Son
GETS NOTES FROM BOOK
H. D. Ulrichs and Martin Ul
richs Arrested for an As
sault With Deadly Weapon
Attorney Joseph F. Coffey had an ex
citing experience in the office of the
Columbia Brewing Company at 326
Clementina street yesterday afternoon.
As a result he had H. D. Ulrichs, the
president and general manager, and
Martin Ulrichs, his son, arrested on a
charge of assault with a deadly
weapon. They were booked at the City
Prison and each released on $25 cash
bail fixed by Police Judge Cabaniss.
Coffey is a stockholder in the com
pany and is also attorney for August
F. Meyer, another stockholder, who
had President Ulrichs arrested on
Wednesday for assault and refusing
to show the cash book to a stockholder
of the company. The cases are pend
ing before, Police Judge Fritz.
Coffey called at the offlce yesterday
afternoon and asked permission to ex
. amine the books. No objection was
offered and he took a memorandum of
" data that he thought would be useful
to him.
In telling his story of what occurred
afterward Coffey said: "Young Ul
richs asked me what I had written
down. I told him that was my busi
â¢ ness. He demanded the paper which I
held in my hand and when I refused
he seized me by the throat and forced
me into a corner. The father grabbed
hold of a club and struck 'me on the
arm with it. Young Ulrichs continued
to choke me and asked his father if he
had got the paper. He said he had not.
Young Ulrichs released his hold of me
and I managed to shove the paper
Into my trousers pocket.
"I was again seized by the throat by
young Ulrichs and thrown over a light
ed stove. I was nearly all out when
I was again released. I told them I
would keep the paper and the old man
ran out of the offlce, saying he would
get a policeman and have me arrested.
The young man, who is six feet tall and |
"weighs about 180 pounds, locked the j
offlce door to keep me in. I called up
Captain Spillane and asked him to send
a policeman at once. The officer came
.and I had the father and son arrested.
The stockholders are not satisfied with
â¢ the way the brewery is being managed
by the father and son and we are de
. termined to get at the facts."
Ulrichs and his son said they merely
tried to put Coffey out and there was a
little scuffle. They are represented by

SIDEWALK ORATOR DECLARES
HIS LANGUAGE WAS
NOT UNLAWFUL.
Policeman Thinks Otherwise
Bat the Words Did Not Aeooy as
Did the Maeaifer of Delivery.
Adolf Alfonso, youthful of appear
ance and earnest of manner, denied
that he was advocating anarchy when
Patrolman Nelson plucked him from
the midst of an approving auditory on
Clay street, between Montgomery and
Kearny. He deplored the apparent in
ability of a uniformed Incarnation of
the law to discern as between the ten
ets of the red flag cult and the doc
trines of the less belligerent creed of
socialism. 'Twas true, as specified in
the formal complaint accusing him of
peace disturbance, that he had ad
dressed a concourse of willing listeners
on a public thoroughfare, but he de
fied the man who arrested him to prove
that his preachments were of an Incen
diary character.
Then the policeman averred that it
was less what Mr. Alfonso had said
I than his manner of saying It and his
| obstruction of the sidewalk that pro-
I yoked the arrest. He (the officer) would
j confess that he was not profoundly
versed in the principles of either an
; archy or socialismâmuch less so In the
| fine distinctions that divide those
j schools of discontent. His duty of dis
i ciplining the "wine bums" that infest
l his beatâof which element Mr. Alfon
so's audience was largely composedâ
prevented his study of such abstract
questions. Perhaps If Mr. Alfonso were
a policeman assigned to regulate the
"wine joints" and their habitues he
might have less time to devote to ora
tory and the absorption of food there
for. What the officer did hear Mr. Al
fonso say was denunciatory of 'almost
| everybody and everything connected
j with the governmental institutions of
â  this country, fncludlng the Army and
navy, the courts and the police. He
had said that If less money were spent
in the maintenance of soldiers and sall-
I ors and Judges and "cops" there would
be more money spent on the malnten
i ance of the people, and the necessity
lof "slaving" for a livelihood would be
j correspondingly lessened. His utter
! ances were heartily applauded by men
I who to the officer's certain knowledge
I for many years had not earned a Hve
! lihood, but begged, borrowed or stole
I all they ate, drank or wore. The pollce
i man felt that any doctrine approved by
j such persons could not be promotive of
: society's best Interests.
Mr. Alfonso acknowledged that he
i had no regular employment, also that
he had spent much time in the perusal
of literature and In the listening to dis
courses concordant with the views he
expressed when Patrolman Nelson
scooped him In. His oratorical gift,
| Mr. Alfonso added, was Inherent, and
i its development had not been handi
j capped by his acquirement of a thor
i ough education in either political econ
! omy or the science of government.
Judge Cabanlss then ruled that In
\ ADVERTISEMENTS.
GJ! IR ARD Bhhi *&~
BY JAMES C. CRAWFORD.
what the prosecution had testified there
was no proof that the defendant had
advocated real anarchy, which means
destruction of both life and property in
the attainment of its impractical ends.
So the orator was dismissed and ad
vised to shift his forum from Patrol
man Nelson's beat.

Joseph Smith was held without bail
to answer in the Superior Court the
charge of having murdered Joseph Mc-
Gowan. His attorneys' motion that
bail be allowed was declined by Judge
Mogan, who remarked that the testi
mony showed Smith was the aggressor
throughout and that his killing of Mc-
Gowan was "cold-blooded assassina
tion." Tbe aged defendant displayed ho
feeling whatever while the magistrate
was speaking, but sat at the attorneys'
table and calmly brushed dust specks
from the lapels of his coat.
* â¢ â¢
In the department graced by Judge
Mogan's presidency the 300 block of
Prospect avenue made its first Police
Court appearance this year. During
the preceding twelvemonth It figured
several times before each of the four
magistrates, and the testimony heard
yesterday afforded warrant for a pre
diction that Its best previous record
will be eclipsed during the twelvemonth
to come. The feud that Involves almost
every resident of the block seems to ac
quire strength with age.
Miss Pearl Caflish is the complain
ant this time. It is her fourth ap
pearance In that role, and she has also
posed two or three times as a defend
ant. Her age is 16, but she has had
enough Police Court experience to sat
isfy an octogenarian lover of that
game. Bailiff Hlckey thinks she is al
ready enslaved by the habit. The per
son accused by* her is Charles Sink, a
commercial tourist who owns a dog,
and It was for "sicking" the animal at
her that Miss Caflish haled him into
court. <
"He done it last New Tear's day,"
Miss Caflish testified. "I was attendln'
to me own business, an' not sayln'
nothln' to nobody, when he sicked the
Jog at me."
It was only her superior agility. Miss
Caflish added, that saved her from
being bitten by the pursuing canine,
svhich had been angered to frenzy by
ts master's voice. She opined, fur
thermore, that the attack was a de
velopment of a conspiracy having for
* j.:
its object her removal from the block.
In other words, Mr. Sink was not act
ing entirely on his own Initiative when
he encouraged the dog to chase and
bite her.
Judge Mogan continued the case till
January 25, and wearily expressed a
hope that the interim might establish
something remotely akin to harmony
on the 300 block of Prospect avenue.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1905.
MAKES ATTEMPT
TO BREAK JAIL
While Awaiting Extradition
on Grand Larceny Charge
A. A. Kratz Gets Desperate
OREGON BOOT IS SENT
Detective T. B. Gibson Is Ex
pected to Arrive To-Day
With Him From Mexico
Detective T. B. Gibson is expected to
arrive to-day from Mexico with A. A.
Kratz, who is to -be tried in Judge
Dunne's court on a charge of grand
larceny. Kratz's brother, William, was
j vice president of the Donandt-Kratz
i Company, and on February 13, 1903,
disappeared, taking with him over
$16,000 belonging to the company which
he had collected. A. A. Krktz was with
him at the office when he |eft with the
coin. William was arrested in Kansas
City. He was brought back, tried,
convicted and sentenced to serve five
years in San Quentin.
A. A. Kratz was arrested in this city.
He was out on $10,000 bonds and his
trial had been definitely set for Sep
. tember 17 last. When the case was
j called on that date it was reported that
i Kratz had been drowned while bathing
at Long Beach about a week before.
Affidavits were filed with the court
| from employes at the bathing estab
lishment and others, but doubts were
expressed and Judge Dunne forfeited
Kratz's bonds and issued a bench war
rant for his arrest.
i About a month later Kratz was ar
rested at Ronquillo, Sonora, Mexico,
and on October 28 Gibson left with the
papers for his extradition. Kratz
fought his extradition, mit on Decem
! ber 30 a dispatch was received from
Gibson that he would leave on Janu
ary 4 with his prisoner. In his dis- \
patch he said that Kratz, becoming
desperate, had attacked the guard at
the jail at Ronquillo and had attempted
to make his escape. Gibson suggested
that an "Oregon boot" be forwarded to
him, as he felt convinced that Kratz
would attempt to escape. It was sent
that evening.
The case was called In Judge Dunne's
court on December 30 for trial and by
consent was continued till January 13.

MAYOR DELAYS
APPOINTMENTS
His Failure to Announce
Selections Due to Fight
Over Police Commissioner
CANDIDATES NUMEROUS
Schmitz May Resign From'
Works Board to Get Long
Term for the Same Office
Mayor Schmitz did not officially an
nounce the names of the new mem
bers of the various municipal boards
and commissions yesterday afternoon,
as he said he would. The Mayor's de
lay was caused by the struggle which
is on for the Police Commlssionership.
He would like to give the place to
John O'Brien, a relative by marriage,
and a warm personal friend. Abe
Ruef Is standing behind Dr. Joseph
Pohelm, present member of the Health
Board, while Dr. George Drucker is
backed by heavy influences though
not by the "Horses and Carts" as in
advertently stated in these columns.
Golden M. Roy, a Kearny street optic
ian, has developed formidable politi
cal strength and the wise ones are
looking on him to secure the plum.
Thomas F. Egan yesterday filed
with the Mayor his official bonds for
the office of Commissioner of Public
Works, he having been given instruc
tions to that effect. This confirms his
appointment to the post as predicted
by The Call for some time past. Egan
furnished four personal sureties for
$25,000 In view of the notice of a
surety company that it desires to
withdraw from the bonds of Commis
sioners Schmitz and Maestretti be
cause it considers the risk too great
owing to a Superior Court decision
that the commissioners are individual
ly responsible for accidents from de
fective streets and sidewalks.
The latest rumor is that David J.
Oliver Jr. will be appointed Park
Commissioner temporarily pending the
prospective resignation of Civil Ser
vice Commissioner Rogers, who will
probably go to Washington with Con
gressman-elect Hayes next March. In
that event Oliver will be Rogers* sue- j
cessor. The gossips are figuring that i
Oliver is being seriously considered
for Police Commissioner and there I
may be some grounds for this sur
mise. William H. Metson is a strong
candidate for Park Commissioner.
Many rumors were rife yesterday,
one being that Herbert Schmitz would
resign as Commissioner of Works to i
make way for School Director Boyle.
Bchmtts himself denied that such was

BIG CLOTHING FIRM
TO OPEN NEW STORE
S. N. Wood & Co. Take Ixmg Lease
to a Portion of the Flood
Building.
j Negotiations that have been pend
; ing for several weeks between the
agents of the new Flood building and
the representatives of S. N. Wood &
' Co., the Market street clothiers, re
sulted yesterday in
firm obtaining a ten year lease to a
large portion of the magnificent struc
ture, which is on the eve of its com
pletion. Under the contract that firm
will occupy tha northwest corner of
the big building, including eighty-five
feet frontage on Ellis street and one
hundred feot on Powell. The store
space is to be elaborately finished and
furnished. This establishment will
be operated in conjunction with the
firm's other big store on Market
street.
The lease also conveys to S. N.
Wood & Co. the use of the entire sec
ond floor of the new building for the
same period of years. A part of this
floor will be occupied by the clothing
firm and the remainder sublet to va
rious tenants.
WANTS CARS EQUIPPED
WITH LIFTING JACKS
Stephen Byrne Petitions Supervisors
to Compel Street Railroads to In
stall Devices for Accidents.
Stephen Byrne yesterday petitioned
I the Supervisors to compel street rail
road companies to equip their cars
with lifting jacks. Byrne said he wit
nessed an accident where the victim,
a little boy, was compelled to remain
! under the car thirty minutes until
lifting jacks were borrowed from a
I near-by machine shop. Again Byrne
says that on Monday last a 3-year-old
child was killed on Mission street near
Twenty-third and the frantic parents
were compelled to stand by for half
an hour until lifting jacks could be
brought from the car house at Twen
ty-ninth street. Byrne says that these
sights impel him for the cause of hu
manity to request that some action be
i taken.
